Thesis statement 
Enhancing Evacuations and Challenging Perceptions of Individuals with Autism Spectrum Disorders 
Hypothesis: Qualitative insights will reveal key barriers and facilitators influencing the psychological 
adaptation of individuals with autism spectrum disorder during evacuations. 
Methodology: 
• 
Participants: Autistic teens and adults 
• 
Data Collection: In-depth semi-structured interviews. 
• 
Data Analysis: Thematic analysis to identify common barriers (e.g., sensory challenges, 
communication difficulties) and facilitators (e.g., experience, personalized support, sensory-friendly 
environments) to psychological adaptation. 
The research study incorporates a mixed methods approach of quantitative and qualitative data collection 
methods, including surveys and semi structured interviews. The aim is to provide a comprehensive 
understanding of the factors influencing psychological adaptation during evacuations for autistic individuals 
and investigate the development of tailored strategies and interventions for improved outcomes. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:5==
 
 
4 
Abstract 
The psychological adaptation of individuals with cognitive impairments1, particular those related to autism, 
during emergency situations, specifically evacuations during fire, presents unique challenges and requires 
special considerations (Bukvic et al., 2021; Hostetter & Naser, 2022). This thesis aims to explore the factors 
influencing psychological adaptation, during evacuations, in the population, often perceived as cognitively 
impaired. By identifying potential barriers and facilitators to their adaptation, strategies and interventions to 
enhance their successful evacuation outcomes may be proposed. Thereby transforming perspectives on 
individuals traditionally labelled cognitive impaired and revolutionize the way they are assisted during 
emergencies such as evacuation scenarios.  
 
Psychological adaptation is an important factor in the successful evacuation process. As individuals' ability to 
cope with stress, uncertainty, and challenges during evacuations significantly impacts their overall well-being 
and decision-making, highlighting the importance of emotional regulation, problem-solving skills, social 
support, fostering resilience, and effective information processing in promoting psychological adaptability 
during evacuation scenarios. 
 
However, the question of psychological adaptability in evacuation during fires is not an easy one. Initially it 
may appear binary – either one is adaptable, or one is not. Categorising people this way may be the norm 
(Jones et al., 2021). However, this perspective may not always serve the collective advantage. 
Yet, when considering people who would typically be regarded as less (or not) psychological adaptable, such 
as people with autism, their experiences are often oversimplified. They tend to be categorized uniformly, 
assuming they are inherently unadaptable.  
 
Studies have shown that, especially children, with disabilities (physical as well as cognitive) are thought to 
have an increased risk of injuries (Shi et al., 2011). Therefore, there is a pressing need for deeper insight into 
psychological adaptability in the cognitive “impaired”. By investigation autism in relation to evacuation, a 
better understanding of the impact of cognitive differences on successful evacuation outcomes can be 
obtained. This exploration could lead to a paradigm shift, challenging preconceptions and fostering more 
effective evacuation scenarios for all individuals regardless of their cognitive profiles.  
 
1 Impairments typically refers to a physical, cognitive, or sensory limitation within the individual, while disability refers 
to the societal and environmental barriers that restrict and individual’s full participation due to their impairments. Both 
terms are used in the paper.

1.Introduction 
This chapter explores the evolution of research into human behavior and evacuation during fires, tracing its 
roots back to the mid-20th century. In the mid-20th century pivotal incidents sparked initial interest in 
emergency response behaviors, leading to foundational studies in fire safety protocols. Over the decades, 
research have expanded to consider broader psychological and social factors, such as decision making and 
the impact of sensory impairment on evacuation dynamics. The focus has progressively shifted towards 
understanding and supporting individuals to enhance inclusivity and effectiveness in evacuation procedures.  
 
1.1 Background and Context 
Research into human behavior and evacuation in fires has advanced considerably since the beginning in the 
mid-20th century. Here the tragic incidents of major fires like the Cocoanut Grove fire in 1942 (D. Blossom, 
2007) and the Winecoff Hotel fire in 1946 (A. Arewa et al., 2021) initiated interest in understanding how 
people respond and evacuate during such emergencies. These events are credited with the start of the early 
stages of studying normal human behavior in fires and the development of fire safety protocols. 
 
In the 1970s and 1980s, the study of human behavior expanded beyond traditional evacuation behavior to 
include new perspectives. There was a quest to understand behavior from more interdisciplinary angles. Here 
researchers began to dive deeper into factors like non movers, panic, decision making, social dynamics and 
cognitive processes during evacuations (Bryan, 1983; Hans, 1974; Quarantelli, 1975, 1980; Tong & Canter, 
1985). Also including sensory impairment introduced by the presence of smoke and how the presence of HCN 
and CO could increase cognitive impairment (Hedges et al., 2019). These developments have helped shape 
contemporary approaches and has led to a more comprehensive understanding of how people behave in 
complex fire situations. Although it may seem as there is more of an understanding of the performance of 
people with situational disability as opposed to those with historical limitations, such as retardation. 
 
It was not until 1980s and 1990s that the research into more specific historical cognitive impairment and 
evacuation began to gain attention in the field of emergency management and fire safety. This focus emerged 
as experts realized that individuals with cognitive impairment, such as Alzheimer’s (Namazi et al., 1989), 
retardation (Rae et al., n.d.; Reed, 1989; Rowe & Kedesdy, 1988) and developmental disabilities (including 
Downs syndrome and autism spectrum disorder) (Holburn & Dougher, 1985; Israel et al., 1993; Garcia et al., 
2016) faced unique challenges during evacuations. Understanding how these individuals respond to 
==PAGE:9==
 
 
8 
emergency situations and developing strategies to assist them became important for creating inclusive and 
effective evacuation plans.  
 
In Denmark an increasing percentage of the population has become diagnosed with autism spectrum 
disorder (ASD). The number for children between 0-17 years with ASD is estimated by Landspatientregisteret 
(LPR) to be around 1,67%. Although during the years 1980-2018 the prevalence in the 16-year old’s have 
increased from 0.09% to 2.80% and for now, no longitudinal data have shown that this number have 
stabilized (Schendel et al., 2018). Studies have shown that 7% of children with ASD lives without their parents 
vs. only 2% of the rest of the children in Denmark (Social og Indenrigsministeriet 2020, §71-tilsyn). This means 
that in Denmark in 2018, 1107 children with ASD were placed fulltime outside their home in a care facility. 
The number of children who are in special all-day schools has not been identified. 
Even so, this means that there is a growing group of individuals who are not included in how evacuation is  
typically viewed – or at least individuals might be expected to differ from current expectations. This needs to 
change and this study aims to start that change. 
 
1.2 Hypotheses and Research Objectives 
Individuals with ASD experience distinct challenges during evacuations that impact their ability for 
psychological adaptation. Qualitative and quantitative insight from in-depth semi structured interviews with 
individuals with ASD will reveal key barriers and facilitators influencing their psychological adaptations during 
evacuations. Specifically, it is hypothesized that sensory challenges, communication difficulties, and 
environmental stressors will emerge as common barriers to psychological adaptation, while personalized 
support, sensory friendly environments, routines, and effective communication strategies will be identified 
as facilitators. Furthermore, it is hypothesized that the exploration of these factors and the development of 
tailored strategies and interventions will improve psychological adaptation and successful evacuation 
outcomes for autistic teens and adults during emergency situations. The study highlights the need to move 
beyond a simplistic view of autism and psychological adaptability during evacuations. 
 
The research objective aims to identify common patterns in sensory sensitivities, communication abilities, 
and social interactions among participants with ASD, and hypothesize how these factors influence their 
behavior during routine and emergency situations. The study employs qualitative interviews and qualitative 
questionnaires to gain insight into the adaptive strategies and challenges faced by these individuals, 
enhancing our understanding of ASD in the context of evacuation and informing targeted interventions for 
improved emergency preparedness. 
==PAGE:10==
 
 
9 
 
The research objective can be divided into the following subobjectives: 
• 
To explore the factors influencing psychological adaptation in people with ASD, during evacuations, 
particularly in emergency situations such as fires.  
• 
To identify common barriers and facilitators to psychological adaptation experienced by autistic 
adults during evacuation. 
• 
To examine the role, in the literature, of personalized support, sensory friendly environments, and 
effective communication strategies in promoting psychological adaptation during evacuations for 
autistic adults.  
• 
To propose tailored strategies and interventions to enhance the psychological adaptation and 
successful evacuation outcomes during emergency evacuations situations.  
• 
To challenge perceptions of individuals with cognitive disability as inherently less adaptable and 
promote more nuanced understanding of psychological adaptation in autistic individuals during 
evacuations. 
 
1.3 Significance of the study 
This study aims to explore the lived experience of youth/adults with ASD and their experiences and thoughts 
of evacuation, a currently underexplored area. Existing research have focused on general adaptation or 
challenges faced by individuals with cognitive impairment (Choi et al., 2019), but an in-depth exploration of 
the specific experience and perspective in evacuation scenarios such as fires is lacking. This study aims to 
contribute new insight to this gap in knowledge. 
 
By understanding their experience and the perspectives influencing individuals with ASD, this study may lead 
the development of targeted interventions and strategies. This could involve: 
 
• 
Designing personalized support systems during emergencies. 
• 
Implementing environmental modifications to improve accessibility and reduce sensory overload. 
• 
Training evacuations personnel on effective communication strategies with autistic individuals. 
• 
Developing educational programs for autistic adults on fire safety and evacuation procedures.  
 
By addressing the unique challenges faced by people with ASD and ensuring their llbeing during evacuations, 
this study may contribute improve overall evacuation outcomes for this populations and may lead to 
==PAGE:11==
 
 
10 
increased safety and well-being, enhanced decision making and coping abilities and reduced psychological 
distress and anxiety for individuals with ASD during emergencies. 
 
Hopefully this research will also contribute to a broader understanding of the needs and experiences of 
individuals with ASD during emergencies. This may foster greater social inclusion by raising awareness of the 
challenges faced by individuals with ASD. It may encourage a shift towards inclusive emergency preparedness 
that accommodate more diverse needs and promote empathy and understanding towards specific 
experiences of individuals with ASD in society. 
 
In conclusion, this study holds significant theoretical, practical, and social significance and it has the potential 
to advance knowledge, improve evacuation outcomes, and promote social inclusion for individuals with ASD, 
ultimately contributing to a more inclusive and accessible society for all. 
 
1.4 Scope  
This study entered on the exploration of psychological experiences and adaptations of individuals with ASD 
in the context of evacuation scenarios, with a special focus on fire emergencies. The research is designed to 
provide comprehensive understanding of how individuals with ASD perceive and react to demands of 
evacuation before they occur, highlighting both the challenges and supportive factors that influence their 
psychological state. 
 
To achieve these objectives, the study employs a mixed-method approach, integrating qualitative insights 
with quantitative data. Semi-structured interviews are used to gather in-depth personal narratives, with 
more detailed accounts of individual experiences. These qualitative findings are then supplemented with 
data from the Likert scale part of the questionnaires, which quantify the participants’ perceptions and 
responses, providing a broader perspective on the psychological dimensions of evacuation preparedness 
among this population. 
 
The population under investigation includes autistic individuals aged 14-35, all of whom have received a 
formal diagnosis of ASD. This range allows the study to align with previous studies of young adults and 
capturing a diverse array of experiences and developmental contexts. 
  
The primary phenomenon of interest in this study is the psychological adaptation of individuals with ASD in 
anticipation of fire evacuations. Within this framework, the study aims to identify and understand key 
==PAGE:12==
 
 
11 
barriers and facilitators that impact this process. Barriers such as sensory overload, communication 
challenges, visual environments, and lack of proper support are explored in detail, as these factors are known 
to significantly affect the ability to individuals with autism to effectively engage with and respond to 
situations in general and more specifically emergency situations. 
 
Conversely, the study also investigates facilitators that can enhance psychological adaptation, such as 
personalized support, accessible environments, and the provision of clear and repeated communication. 
These elements are critical in mitigating the challenges faced by individuals with ASD during evacuations and 
are considered essential for fostering a more positive and effective response to emergency scenarios. 
 
Additionally, the research delves into the subjective experience, emotions, and meanings that participants 
associate with evacuation scenarios. This aspect of the study provides valuable insights into the internal 
psychological processes that underlie their behavior and reactions during such high stress events. By 
examining these subjective elements, the study seeks to contribute to a more holistic understanding of the 
psychological needs and adaptation of individuals with ASD in the context of emergency preparedness and 
response. 
 
Overall, this study aims to fill a gap in the existing literature by providing nuanced exploration of the 
psychological factors influencing evacuation preparedness among individuals with ASD, offering important 
implications for the development of more effective and supportive evacuation strategies tailored to this 
population. 
 
2.Literature review 
The purpose of the  literature review is to provide a comprehensive overview of existing research that informs 
the current study’s exploration of the psychological experiences and adaptations of individuals with ASD in 
general and with regards to emergency situations if possible. This section synthesizes relevant findings from 
previous studies, highlighting the theoretical frameworks, empirical evidence, and methodological 
approaches that have shaped our understanding of the intersection between autism, psychological 
adaptation and emergency preparedness. 
Given the complex nature of autism and its impact on cognitive and emotional functioning, the literature 
review will examine a broad range of studies, including those focused on sensory processing, communication 
==PAGE:13==
 
 
12 
barriers, and stress responses in people with autism. Additionally, the review will address how these factors 
influence behavior and decision-making during high stress. 
  
This review will be organized thematically, beginning with the understanding of how ASD have developed, 
general theories and models of psychological adaptation in emergencies, followed by a focus on autism-
specific research.  
 
2.1 Autism Spectrum Disorder (ASD) – definition and conceptualization 
This chapter outlines the evolving understanding of ASD, tracing its classification from rigid subtypes to a 
broader spectrum framework. The transition from DSM-IV (5th ed.; DSM-5; American Psychiatric Association, 
2013 ) and ICD-10’s (International Classification of Diseases pre-2018)  categorical approach to the more 
nuanced spectrum model in DSM-5 and ICD-11 reflects a deeper comprehension of the complexities of ASD. 
This shift in acknowledges the diverse manifestation of ASD and aims to improve diagnostic accuracy and 
personalized care for those affected.  
2.1.1 The Definition 
Autism Spectrum Disorder (ASD) is a neurodevelopmental disorder. Defining ASD is primarily done using two 
classification systems: The Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders (DSM) and the World Health 
Organisation (WHO) International Classification of Diseases (ICD). The DSM is primarily focused on mental 
disorders, but the ICD includes both mental and physical conditions. Some would argue that the DSM favors 
a more categorical approach to mental disorders and the ICD a more holistic understanding of health 
conditions allowing for a broader assessment of the impact on individuals lives. 
 
Previously, in the DSM, ASD was viewed as a relatively narrow and well-defined condition characterized by 
qualitative impairments in: 
• 
Social interactions (4 items) 
o Marked impairment in the use of multiple nonverbal behaviors such as eye--toeye gaze, 
facial expressions, body postures, and gestures to regulate social interactions 
o Failure to develop peer relationships appropriate to developmental level 
o Lack of spontaneous seeking to share enjoyment, interests or achievements with other 
people (e.g., by a lack of showing, bringing or pointing out objects of interest) 
o Lack of social or emotional reciprocity 
• 
Communication (4 items)  
==PAGE:14==
 
 
13 
o Delay in, or total lack of, the development of spoken language (not accompanied by an 
attempt to compensate through alternative modes of communication such as gesture or 
mime) 
o In individuals with adequate speech, marked impairment in the ability to initiate or sustain 
a conversation with others 
o Stereotyped and repetitive use of language or idiosyncratic language 
o Lack of varied, spontaneous, make-believe play or social imitative play appropriate to 
developmental level. 
• 
Restricted/repetitive/stereotyped behavior and activities (4 items)  
o Encompassing preoccupation with one or more stereotyped and restricted patterns of 
interest that is abnormal either in intensity or focus 
o Apparently inflexible adherence to specific nonfunctional routines or rituals 
o Stereotyped and repetitive motor mannerisms (e.g., hand or finger flapping or twisting, or 
complex whole-body movements) 
o Persistent preoccupation with parts of objects 
(Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders, 4th ed.; DSM-IV; American Psychiatric 
Association, 2000).  
 
To have the diagnosis a total of six or more items would be required. Delay or abnormal functioning of at 
least one area should have onset prior to 3 years of age and the impairment should not be accounted for by 
Rett’s disorder or Childhood Disintegrative disorder (CDD- Hellers).  Autism was often diagnosed as one of 4 
distinct subtypes, such as Autistic disorder, Asperger’s syndrome, Childhood Disintegrative disorder, and 
Pervasive Developmental Disorder. The criteria when diagnosing these subtypes were quite rigid and often, 
they were used (or supposed to be used) with specific cutoff points, making it more challenging to capture 
the full variation seen in individuals with autism. It was often seen that the diagnoses were not consistently 
applied across clinics and treatment centers(Rosen et al., 2021). 
 
In the ICD-10 ASD fell under the category of Pervasive Developmental disorders and was coded as F84. This 
category included several conditions characterized by severe and pervasive impairment in various areas of 
development. Here the subtypes included: 
• 
Childhood Autism (F84.0): Includes marked impairment in social interactions, communications and 
imaginative play before the age of three. Also there should be restricted interest and stereotypical 
behavior. This was also known as the classic form of autism. 
==PAGE:15==
 
 
14 
• 
Atypical Autism (F84.1): Cases not fully meeting the criteria for F84.0 but still exhibit specific 
features of autism. 
• 
Other Pervasive Developmental Disorders (F84.8): More of an umbrella type condition that fell 
under the Pervasive developmental Disorder but did not specifically meet the criteria for F84.0 or 
F84.1) 
 
In more recent years, the understanding of ASD has evolved to recognize it as a spectrum, where a wide 
range of characteristics, strengths and challenges may vary greatly from person to person. The DSM-V, 
eliminated separate subtypes an introduced a unified category of ASD. This change was aimed at better 
capturing the diversity and complexity of the condition (Gensler, 2012).  
 
In 2018 ICD-11 followed suit, again also reflecting the recognition of autism as a spectrum disorder with 
varying levels of severity and functional impact. ASD is now classified under Disorders of Neuro 
developmental Origin Section 6D and has been assigned the code 6D10 (ICD-11). 
 
For both DSM-5 and ICD-11 ASD is described as a developmental disorder characterized by persistent 
difficulties in the following areas: 
• 
Social Communication and Social Interaction: The focus here is difficulties in understanding and 
using verbal and nonverbal communication for social purposes (having no language or being very 
concrete in language or the understanding of language) challenges with social interactions 
(difficulties in understanding social cues), and problems with developing and maintaining 
relationships (often due to the lack of engaging in reciprocal social interactions). 
• 
Restricted and Repetitive Behaviors: This involves engaging in repetitive motor movements or 
behaviors (flapping of hands, head bopping) having rigid routines (insistence on sameness), 
exhibiting intense interest (special or restricted interests), and hyper- or hypo-reactivity to sensory 
input.  
 
The shift from more rigid subtypes to a spectrum disorder view, acknowledges that individuals with ASD can 
have varying degrees of impairment in these domains, leading to a more personalized understanding of the 
disorder. Additionally, there is also a growing appreciation for the strengths and talents that many individuals 
with ASD possess.   
==PAGE:16==
 
 
15 
2.1.2 The Nuanced Picture 
ASD is recognized as a complex neurodevelopmental condition, however, the narrative around autism, as 
seen above, have often focused solely on the challenges. Fortunately, a growing body of research reveals a 
more nuanced picture of strengths and abilities that individuals with ASD possess.  
 
One such strength is within perception. Individuals with ASD often demonstrate a strong attention to details 
and heightened ability to focus on and process visual details on specific tasks or topics of interest. Research 
have shown that individuals with ASD often excel in tasks requiring attention to detail, such as visual search 
tasks and pattern recognition (O’riordan et al., 2001; Plaisted et al., 1998).  This has also been seen to extend 
to auditory memory and the ability to discern subtle variations in sound, as seen in the study by Bonnel  
(Bonnel et al., 2010) , although later reviews of autists ability to accurately process and interpret auditory 
information has been seen to generally be difficult and often suggest that complex auditory information is 
often impaired(O’Connor, 2012). 
 
Hyper focus or persistence have often been demonstrated in individuals with ASD, within their area of 
interest. The hyper focus combined with persistence/attentional strengths have been seen to lead to high 
levels of productivity and achievement in areas of interest for high functioning individuals with autist. 
Research has documented instances of intense focus and persistence in individuals with ASD, particularly in 
preferred activities (Dupuis et al., 2022). It has also been documented that a greater than normal capacity 
for information processing may be prevalent (Remington et al., 2012).  
 
Memory has also been studied with individuals with ASD, and exceptional memory skills have been observed 
in areas of personal interest or expertise. Frequently individuals with ASD have demonstrated strong rote 
memory and unique learning styles. Rote memory entails memory for large amounts of information and 
material without much reference to meaning, emotions, or contexts. It is often learned by repetition and 
without full comprehension (as tracked by eye movement), or attention to the memorized (such as a 
phonebook, numberplates, spelling) and prompted by external cues (Ritvo et al., 2013).  Some evidence also 
suggests that in High Functioning Autists (HFA) there are intact and normal memory functions (Ozonoff & 
Strayer, 2001; Williams et al., 2006). 
 
Furthermore, individuals with ASD often excel in logical and analytical thinking, and for HFA they are often 
seen in field such as science, technology, engineering and mathematics domain (STEM) (Wei et al., 2013). 
Research has shown that individuals with ASD may demonstrate advanced mathematical abilities (Baron-
==PAGE:17==
 
 
16 
Cohen et al., 2007), logical problem-solving skills (not interpersonal), and pattern recognition abilities (Crespi, 
2021) 
 
Another very prevalent trait in individuals with ASD is rigidity or rituals. In which strict adherence to rules is 
also included. Generally, it may be seen that their tendency to, very strictly and rigid, follow rules, routines 
and rituals can cause problems, as they lack the plasticity that is often needed in normal day life and day to 
day social interactions (Petrolini et al., 2023). Although the adherence to learned rules may also be very 
beneficial as this in conjunction with memory may strengthen learned information. Even rigid thinking may 
be used in a positive way, as the behavior of the person is better predicted and foreseen even in a changing 
environment. 
 
In summary ASD is a neurodevelopmental condition marked by different challenges. Historically, ASD was 
classified into rigid subtypes like Autistic Disorder, Asperger’s Syndrome, and Childhood Disintegrative 
Disorder, as seen in the DSM-IV and ICD-10. These distinctions often failed to capture the full range of 
symptoms and were inconsistently applied across clinical settings. The DSM-5 and ICD-11 shifted to a broader 
spectrum model, recognizing the varying severity and functional impact of ASD, leading to a more flexible 
and personalized understanding of the disorder. 
 
ASD’s core disabilities include difficulties in verbal and nonverbal communication, interpreting social clues, 
and rigid, repetitive behaviors. However, recent research emphasizes strengths in many individuals with ASD, 
such as attention to detail, exceptional memory, hyperfocus, and persistence. These abilities often result in 
achievements in areas such as pattern recognition, problem-solving, and STEM fields. The recognition of both 
challenges and strengths in ASD promotes a more comprehensive and individualized approach to diagnosis 
and support. 
 
2.2 Theoretical Frameworks and Models of Psychological Adaptation  
Psychological adaptation refers to the process by which individuals adjust their cognitive, emotional, and 
behavioral to cope effectively with changes, challenges, or stressors in their environment. It involves the 
ability to regulate thoughts, emotions, and behaviors to maintain a sense of well-being and functioning in 
the face of new or stressful situations (Beck & Weishaar, 1989; Lazarus, 1974).  The framework offers valuable 
insight into the mechanisms of adaptation and may help predict, explain, intervene and promote positive 
psychological coping in individual facing stress and uncertainty in the face of adversity, such as evacuation 
scenarios.  
==PAGE:18==
 
 
17 
 
2.3 Cognitive Disability and Emergency Situations 
When discussing how cognitive disability and emergency situations have been historically viewed and are 
currently understood, it is important to highlight the evolution in both research and practical approaches to 
these issues.  
 
Historically, cognitive disability – whether due to intellectual disabilities, dementia, or developmental 
disorders – were often misunderstood or overlooked in many societal contexts, not just in emergencies. 
These individuals frequently faced exclusion from broader systems designed without their specific needs in 
mind. Educational systems, healthcare access, and even workplace accommodations were/are often 
inadequate or inappropriate for individuals with cognitive challenges. Moreover, stigma surrounding 
cognitive abilities further marginalize this group, resulting in systemic neglect and underrepresentation. The 
predominant view in earlier decades was that individuals with cognitive (or physical) disability was best 
managed through segregation or exclusion from mainstream society. This was also true for most part of the 
19’ century. Equally it was seen that emergency preparedness strategies were largely designed with the 
assumption of neurotypical cognitive functioning, which led to significant gaps in support for those with 
cognitive disability. 
 
2.3.1 The Medical Model of Disability 
In the beginning of the research into 
cognitive disability the focus was very 
much on the medical model of 
disability. The medical model, as 
described by Barnes (2019) views 
disability 
as 
the 
deficiency 
or 
impairment of the individual, that 
needs to be diagnosed, treated, and 
managed by medical professionals. 
With little consideration given to 
broader societal structures or the 
need for systematic change. In this 
model the focus is on the person’s 
Figure 1: The Medical Model of Disability. This is a diagram of the traditional 
Medical Model of Disability, which the social model was developed to challenge 
(Barnes, 2019). 
==PAGE:19==
 
 
18 
medical condition/disability and how to alleviate its effect and hopefully to cure it (Barnes, 2019).  
 
The medical model’s approach has often resulted in people with disabilities being treated as passive 
recipients of care rather than active participants in their own life (or safety) (Figure 1). Disabilities are, in the 
frame of this theory, often seen as inherent within the individual, and the emphasis is on adapting the person 
to fit into a “normal” societal framework, which would inherently lead to evacuations having focus on how 
to manage and not how to include the disabled in plans for evacuation. 
In the context of evacuation preparedness and response, this has led to a focus on managing individuals with 
disabilities rather than integrating their needs into a broader planning process. As an example, evacuation 
plans under this model might prioritize the quick removal of individual with cognitive disability from 
dangerous situations but would not necessarily consider how these individuals could be included in planning 
or given tools to understand and respond to emergencies themselves. 
 
The medical model’s approach can be problematic because it fails to acknowledge the diverse capabilities 
and needs of individuals with disabilities, instead emphasizing a one-size fits all approach that often overlooks 
the importance of inclusion and empowerment (“Disability Rights and Wrongs,” 2007). In essence, the focus 
was on adapting the person with the disability to fit into a “normal” societal framework, which inherently 
excludes them from participating in emergency planning process. 
 
2.3.2 The Social Model of Disability 
Over time, there has been a significant 
shift towards the social model of 
disability (Figure 2), which contrasts 
sharply with the medical model. The 
social 
model 
emphasizes 
that 
disability is not just a problem within 
the individual or entirely the result of 
an individual’s impairment, but may 
be largely shaped by societal barriers, 
attitudes, structures, and physical 
environments. 
According 
to 
this 
model, disability arises from the 
interaction between the person and 
Figure 2: The Social Model of Disability. The Social Model of Disability states that 
the oppression and exclusion people with impairments face is caused by the way 
society is run and organized (Barnes, 2019).. 
==PAGE:20==
 
 
19 
their environment. Barriers such as inaccessible building, lack of accommodation and societal prejudice 
contribute to disabling the individuals (Hostetter & Naser, 2022). Some have also called it the affirmative 
model of disability. Which argues for a “non-tragic” view of disability (Swain & French, 2010). The social 
model emphasizes the need to change barriers, societal structures and create an inclusive society that 
accommodate the diversity of abilities and places the responsibility on society to create an inclusive 
environment where everyone can participate fully (Oliver, 2018).  
 
In the context of the social model of disability, evacuation focuses on creating an inclusive and accessible 
environment that accommodates the diverse needs of individuals, regardless of their abilities. The social 
model recognizes that disabilities are not solely intrinsic to individuals but are often exacerbated by societal 
barriers and inaccessible environments. Using the social model of disability to focus on how society may be 
more inclusive and make evacuation accessible, more knowledge as to how society define and conceptualize 
autism spectrum disorder is needed. This may be found using the theoretical model of psychological 
adaptation, to find barriers and facilitators to ensure equal inclusive and accessible evacuation possibilities. 
 
The shift from exclusionary practices to inclusive, research-informed approaches represent a significant 
advancement in how society views the intersection of cognitive disability. The focus is now on empowering 
individuals with cognitive disability by providing the necessary tools and support to navigate as independently 
as possible, not only in everyday activities, but hopefully also soon in emergency situations. This is also seen 
as Federal Emergency Management Agency (FEMA) has adopted the functional needs approach as a way to 
define disability in relation to the disaster. It is possible to be socially disabled, without being functionally 
disabled (Emergency, 2002; Hostetter & Naser, 2022). 
This shift underscores the need for ongoing research and development of strategies and prioritize inclusion 
and accessibility, ensuring that emergency planning and response efforts are effective for everyone, 
regardless of cognitive ability. This evolution reflects a broader societal commitment to equity and inclusion. 
 
2.4 Psychological Adaptation and Their Influence on Individuals with ASD: Emotional 
Response and Regulation 
In exploring the psychological adaptations of individuals with ASD during evacuations, it is important to 
understand the underlying cognitive and emotional processes that influence how these individuals perceive, 
respond to, and cope with such high-stress events. These processes shape how these individuals perceive, 
react to, and manage the stress of such events. Psychological adaptation in this context is a complex and 
==PAGE:21==
 
 
20 
multifaceted process, and involves a dynamic interplay of various components, each contributing to the 
overall ability to manage and respond to emergency situations. 
 
Evacuations are stressful for everyone but visualize the added challenges for individuals with ASD. Due to the 
characteristic differences in social communications, sensory processing, and cognitive functioning associated 
with ASD; the cognitive appraisal process may differ from that of neurotypical individuals. Elements such as 
sensory overload, change in routines, rigidity and difficulties with communication are all elements that may 
impact their ability to cope. This section explores the factors influencing how individuals with ASD may be 
influenced in their adaptation during evacuations. It aims to understand the unique barriers faced and what 
research says about the factors influencing psychological adaptation and by exploring these factors maybe a 
more inclusive and supportive plan for evacuation may be closer. 
 
Understanding these components provides a comprehensive framework for examining how individuals with 
ASD adapt psychologically to evacuation scenarios, offering possibilities of tailored interventions that can 
support their well-being during such critical events. 
 
2.4.1. Cognitive Appraisal and Reappraisal 
The first step in psychological adaptation during evacuation is to perceive the seriousness of the situation 
and recognize the need to evacuate. This involves understanding the potential risks and hazards prompting 
the evacuation, such as smoke, fire, alarms, or other emergencies.  
Richard Lazarus and Susan Folkman (1974), emphasizes the conscious evaluation of situations to determine 
their significance. Cognitive appraisal is the initial step in this process, a process by which individual evaluate, 
assess, and interpret a situation, to determine its personal urgency, severity, and potential impact of the 
event (Lazarus, 1993). This appraisal is influenced by information from authorities, personal safety 
assessment, and known or available evacuation resources and options. It is considered a fundamental aspect 
of human cognition and plays a crucial role in shaping emotional responses, behavioral reactions, and coping 
strategies. This is also referred to as the primary appraisal. 
 
Cognitive reappraisal is a psychological strategy used to manage and regulate emotions by altering one’s 
interpretation of a situation. This can be done before, during or after an event, both consciously and 
unconsciously. The first step is acknowledging the situation that triggers an emotional response – in this case, 
an evacuation. The initial appraisal is considered more automatic. Cognitive reappraisal challenges the initial 
==PAGE:22==
 
 
21 
evaluation, generating more objective or constructive interpretation of the situation, although there are also 
individual differences in these interpretations (Hendricks & Buchanan, 2016).   
 
Studies using fMRI have shown that successful cognitive reappraisal activates the prefrontal cortex, a brain 
region associated with higher-order cognitive functions and emotional regulation, while reducing activity in 
the amygdala the brain’s fear center, leading to a calmer emotional state (Modinos et al., 2010). 
 
So cognitive reappraisal becomes the individual’s ability to reframe or reinterpret stressful or challenging 
situations in a more positive and manageable way. The benefit of cognitive reappraisal is that by reframing 
negative thoughts, a person may experience less stress, anxiety or anger. Thereby gaining more control over 
reactions to challenging situations, hopefully approaching challenging situations more constructively and 
finding better solutions. This may also involve finding meaning in difficult experiences, adopt a problem-
solving mindset, or adjust one’s expectations and goals  (Yeung et al., 2016). 
 
Understanding cognitive appraisal in individuals with ASD is an emerging field and presents with some 
interesting complexities. For individuals with ASD, the sensory sensitivities and atypical responses to stimuli 
like lights, sounds, or smells may often influence their cognitive appraisal of situations. Research suggests 
that individuals with ASD may perceive threats more intensely in ambiguous situations (Forgeot D’arc, 2011). 
They may perceive certain environments as overwhelming or aversive, leading to heightened stress or anxiety 
(S. A. Green et al., 2012). This could lead to a more negative initial appraisal during evacuation, making it 
difficult for the individual to shift perspective from what they might be doing at the moment or even from 
the initial fear to evacuating. 
 
Other studies suggest that difficulties with social communication and understanding the perspectives of 
others (faces, expressions, speak, intentions, emotions) and understanding social contexts, could impair their 
ability to accurately access the urgency of evacuation situations (C. A. Mazefsky & White, 2014). Individuals 
might misinterpret the urgency of an evacuation situation or struggle to grasp the broader picture of the 
situation (Forgeot D’arc, 2011). 
 
Moreover, rigidity, a common trait in ASD characterized resistance to change and a preference for routines, 
sameness, and predictability, can create challenges in adapting to novel situations like evacuations, 
exacerbating anxiety.  It is therefore seen that individuals with ASD often struggle with cognitive appraisal in 
==PAGE:23==
 
 
22 
situations that involve uncertainty, novelty, or unexpected changes, leading to heightened anxiety or distress 
(South et al., 2007). 
 
As seen in studies by Modios cognitive reappraisal activates the prefrontal cortex (Modinos et al., 2010). 
Research suggests that individuals with ASD may exhibit atypical patterns of prefrontal cortex activation, 
which could contribute to difficulties in the processing of reappraisal (Shalom, 2009; Solomon, Ozonoff, et 
al., 2008). This will not only be seen in executive functioning and cognitive control processes, such as 
planning, attention and multi-tasking, but also what is referred to as theory of mind. Theory of mind is the 
ability to understand and attribute mental states to oneself and others (Frith & Frith, 2003; Kennedy & 
Adolphs, 2012). fMRI studies have reported differences in prefrontal cortex activation during tasks involving 
theory of mind in individuals with ASD compared to neurotypical individuals (Castelli et al., 2002). These 
differences may contribute to difficulties in understanding other perspectives, intentions, and emotions in 
social interactions.  
 
Additional, emotional regulation strategies such as cognitive reappraisal, may not come naturally to 
individuals with ASD due to impaired prefrontal cortex activation.  Without the ability to reframe and reassess 
the situation, the emotional response triggered by the amygdala remains unchecked, resulting in sustained 
stress or panic. These difficulties underscore the need for tailored interventions strategies that focuses on 
sensory management and emotional regulation training for individuals with ASD, especially in emergency 
situations (Begeer et al., 2011).  
 
2.4.2. The Two Path-Way Model 
Emotional response and regulation are critical components of how individuals react to stressful situations, 
such as evacuations and is a key component of psychological adaptation. Evacuations are inherently stressful 
situations, and stress triggers several emotions, such as fear, anger, anxiety, and for some even grief. These 
processes involve not only the immediate emotional reactions to threat but also the strategies used to 
manage and control emotions in a way that allows for effective decision-making and problem solving. In the 
context of evacuation, emotional regulation can mean the difference between successful evacuation and 
heightening panic, or freezing, which could endanger one’s safety. Understanding emotional response and 
regulation in both neurotypical individuals and those with ASD reveals significant differences in how these 
groups might approach and manage emergency situations.  
 
==PAGE:24==
 
 
23 
Emotional regulation refers to the ability to modulate and manage one’s emotional response to stimuli. 
Richard Lazarus and Susan Folkman (1984) where the champions of the conscious evaluation with their 
appraisal model, but LeDoux (1998) describes a model that initially happens without thought. He described   
emotional responses as either automatic or fast and deliberate to stimuli as described by his two pathways 
model, describing the “low road” and the “high road”. The low road is the fast, automatic response is often 
associated with the activation of the amygdala (fear center), a brain structure responsible for detecting 
threats and triggering immediate emotional reactions such as fear or anxiety. This response prepares the 
body to either face the threat (fight) or flee from it (flight). Physiological changes such as increased heart 
rate, heightened alertness, and faster breathing, help the individual prepare for action (LeDoux, 2014). The 
response is often rapid and reflexive, which is beneficial in situations requiring quick action, like emergency 
evacuations.  
 
However, while the amygdala’s reaction may be essential for quick responses, it is often unrefined and 
emotional, sometimes resulting in panic or irrational behavior. A slower, more deliberate response involves 
prefrontal cortex activation, which is associated with higher order cognitive functions such as planning, 
decision making and rational thinking, also referred to as the high road (Gross, 2015). The pre-frontal cortex 
helps regulate the initial response, assesses the situation in more detail, taking to account previous 
experiences, knowledge of the environment, and potential risks, by evaluating the situation more carefully, 
often collaborating with external cues like clear communication from authorities or well-
designed/established evacuation plans. If the individual can activate this pathway, they are more likely to 
make rational decisions and approach the situation in a more controlled manner (Damasio & Damasio, 2012).  
 
Emotional regulation strategies used by neurotypical individuals during stressful situations like evacuations 
may include relaxation techniques (deep breathing, etc.), seeking social support, and distraction can play a 
pivotal role in maintaining psychological balance during a crisis. Effective communication and well-
designed/established evacuation protocols can activate the prefrontal cortex, enabling individuals to make 
more rational decisions rather than being overwhelmed by emotional reactions (Mastracci et al., 2014). By 
successfully managing their emotions, individuals are less likely to experience overwhelming fear or panic, 
which could hinder decision making or cause a fight or flight response such as freeze, during evacuation 
(Gross, 2015). Not letting emotions halt decision making. Also, the ability to express emotions or 
communication in a healthy and controlled manner will help the process of evacuation, not only for the 
individual itself, but for others around them (Mao et al., 2019) . 
 
==PAGE:25==
 
 
24 
While neurotypical individuals may benefit from deliberate, rational processing of the prefrontal cortex to 
calm their emotional responses. Individuals with ASD may rely more heavily on the automatic, reactive 
pathway. As research have shown that individuals with ASD often have atypical activation patterns in this 
brain region, which may impair their ability to regulate emotions and make decisions under stress (Dorit Ben 
Shalom, 2009; Solomon, …, et al., 2008). The ability to regulate emotions during emergencies is often 
compromised due to heightened emotional reactivity and sensory sensitivities. Individuals with ASD often 
have increased sensitivity to stimuli such as loud alarms, flashing alarms, or crowded environments, which 
can amplify the flight-or-fight response (S. Green et al., 2010). This means that they may experience sensory 
overload, during which they may experience even more heightened emotional responses or have 
compromised emotional regulation. Their inability to maintain the psychological equilibrium necessary for 
evacuation may hinder an adequate response, such as relevant decision making. This reliance on the flight-
or-fight response could lead to heightened anxiety, poor decision-making, or even refusal to comply with 
evacuation procedures (C. Mazefsky et al., 2018).   
 
In summary, the LeDoux two-pathway model provides a framework for understanding the dual processes 
that govern emotional responses during stressful events like evacuations. While neurotypical individuals can 
often rely on the activation of prefrontal cortex, cognitive strategies and social support to manage their 
emotions, individuals with ASD face additional challenges. For individuals with ASD, emotional regulation is 
particularly challenging due to heightened sensitivity to stimuli and atypical prefrontal cortex functioning. By 
recognizing these challenges, interventions can be developed through tailored strategies, to better support 
emotional regulations in individuals with ASD during emergency scenarios, ensuring that their psychological 
needs are addresses not only during evacuations, but also before. Then an improvement in the understanding 
of individuals with ASD might be seen, improve outcomes and ensure safer, more effective evacuations for 
everyone.  
 
2.4.3 Effective Decision Making  
Effective decision making involves the ability to weigh options and make logical or rational decisions despite 
uncertainty or pressure. The ability to weigh different options and ideas to identify the best course of action 
in that situation at that time (Jonassen, 2012). Evacuation scenarios pose significant challenges to individuals 
psychological well-being and necessitate effective decision-making for optimal adaptation. Evacuation events 
will most likely trigger the flight-fight-freeze response releasing stress hormones (Cortisol, adrenaline etc.) 
that may negatively impact cognitive functions important to decision making (LeDoux, 2014). The stress of 
an evacuation scenario, can also impair cognitive processes like attention, working memory, and decision 
==PAGE:26==
 
 
25 
making, that can lead to hasty or inaccurate decisions, potentially jeopardizing safety and well-being during 
evacuations (McEwen & Gianaros, 2011).  
 
Individuals often rely on mental shortcuts called heuristics, when under time pressure and stress. This allows 
a person to reduce mental effort needed to make decisions and makes for a fast and accurate way to arrive 
at a conclusion. Heuristics are often based on simplified rules of thumb, and this is also the problem with 
heuristics, that they simplify complex and difficult problems and can lead to biases that hinder optimal 
decision making (Kahneman & Tversky, 2013) 
Biases are a tendency to prefer one thing to another. An example of a bias with regards to evacuation it could 
be an anchoring bias or availability bias (Kahneman & Tversky, 2018; Kinateder et al., 2014). Anchoring bias 
would for example be relying too much on initial information when deciding on evacuation. Such as following 
the crowd and not assessing the situation independently. Availability bias is the overestimation of the 
likelihood of events based on their prominence in our memory. It could be focusing on the danger of exiting 
via the stair after hearing of people getting stuck and dying on stairs during a fire. Even though stairs may be 
a safer space to be, during a fire. 
 
Effective decision making plays a critical role in psychological adaptation during evacuation scenarios. 
Recognizing the impact of stress and cognitive biases, implementing pre-emptive strategies, and utilizing 
available resources can enhance a person’s ability to make good decisions and navigate challenging situations 
with greater resilience and safety. This process may be particularly challenging for individuals with ASD, who 
may or may not struggle with executive functioning tasks (Geurts et al., 2014) . 
 
Cognitive abilities differ from all persons although individuals with pre-existing cognitive disability or lower 
baseline cognitive skills might be more vulnerable to stress induced decision-making deficits during 
evacuations. Individuals with ASD might not have what is considered a traditional cognitive impairment, but 
they are often seen to experience sensory processing differences and may be more susceptible to 
information overload from sights, sounds and tactile information. 
 
Differences in sensory processing is often diagnosed in people with autism. Up to eight subcategories have 
been reported. Such as hyper-, hyperresponsivity, sensory seeking or sensation avoiding (DeBoth & Reynolds, 
2017). Although most often seen is hyperresponsivity.  To ensure effective decision making it is therefore 
important to avoid information overload when preparing a person with autism (as well as any other), for 
evacuation. So, it is important to critically evaluate information that is needed to effectively evacuate 
==PAGE:27==
 
 
26 
(Endsley, 1995). To ensure this it is important that there is a trusted source that may help individuals regulate 
their emotional state and reduce stress-induced cognitive disability, enhancing the possibility for more 
deliberate decision making (Lindell & Prater, 2003). 
In line with the social model of disability system to support decision making can be integrated into building 
infrastructure, it could offer personalized guidance and potentially reducing cognitive overload for individuals 
during evacuations (Wang et al., 2023). 
In conclusion, effective decision-making during evacuation scenarios is a critical component of psychological 
adaptation, particularly under stress. The ability to weigh options and make rational choices can be 
significantly impaired by the fight- flight-freeze response, which compromises cognitive functions like 
attention, memory, and judgement. Heuristics, while useful in reducing mental effort, may introduce biases 
such as anchoring and availability bias, further complicating decision-making in high -pressure environments. 
For individuals with ASD, these challenges are amplified due to sensory processing differences, which 
increase susceptibility to information overload. To mitigate these effects, it is important to reduce cognitive 
and sensory demands by providing clear, structured information using trusted sources to guide decision-
making. Personalized support system, integrated into building infrastructure, can further assist individuals by 
reducing overload and facilitating more deliberate decision-making, ultimately enhancing safety and 
evacuation outcomes. 
 
2.4.4 Adaptive Coping and Problem Solving 
Adaptive coping and problem solving are essential components of psychological adaptation. These processes 
enable individuals to adjust their behavior and strategies when faced with new, stressful, or challenging 
situations, such as during evacuation. Coping effectively requires the ability to integrate new information and 
modify existing plans. According to Lazarus and Folkman (1984), coping is a dynamic process that involves 
learning new skills, changing routine, or adopting alternative strategies that better suit the immediate 
context. The flexibility is essential in situations where the unpredictability of events, demands quick and 
efficient decision-making. Importantly, adaptive comping is not seen as solely an individual effort, but it can 
be facilitated by social or personalized support. 
 
The cognitive processes involved in adaptive coping include problem-solving, planning, and evaluating 
available options, which are necessary for navigating stressful events. Carver et al. (1989) highlighted that 
problem-focused coping, which entails directly addressing the issue causing stress, is particularly effective in 
situations where action can mitigate potential harm, such as evacuating during a fire. This type of coping 
==PAGE:28==
 
 
27 
requires individuals to be receptive to new information and capable of adjusting their responses based on 
evolving circumstances. 
 
In addition to individual cognitive strategies, social support plays a significant role in facilitating adaptive 
coping, particular in high-pressure situations like evacuations. Cohen and Wills (1985) proposed that social 
support acts as a buffer against stress, reducing both its psychological and physiological impacts. Emotional 
support provides individuals with emotional comfort, while practical assistance, and informational resources 
helps with the execution of necessary actions.  
 
Strong social networks have been shown to improve resilience and adaptive comping. Research by Ozbay et 
al. (2007) demonstrated that social support not only alleviates the immediate effects of stress but also 
enhances problem-solving capabilities by providing clarity and guidance in stressful situations. The presence 
of reliable support, whether emotional or informational, significantly impacts the individual’s capacity to 
navigate complex and high-pressure scenarios. 
 
Individuals with ASD often experience unique challenges in adaptive coping due to difficulties with cognitive 
flexibility, social understanding, sensory processing and executive functioning. Geurts et al. (2014) found that 
these individuals may struggle more with changes to routine or unexpected events, leading to heightened 
stress and difficulty in adapting to new demands. Sensory sensitivities and information processing challenges 
further complicate the coping process. 
 
The Person-Environment Fit Model (Lawton & Nahemow, 1973) suggests that an individual’s ability to adapt 
to stressful situations is influenced by the alignment between personal capacities and environmental 
demands. In this context, when the demands of an evacuation environment are in line with an individual’s 
capabilities, the person is more likely to cope effectively with the situation. Although it has also been shown 
that it may sometimes be difficult to identify which goals and objectives that should be addressed with this 
population when it comes to a more general educational intervention  (Wilczynski et al., 2007) , this may not 
the case when it comes to evacuation where the goals and objective are more specific. 
 
Overall, psychological adaptation is a continuous and dynamic process that may vary across individuals and 
situations. It is influenced by many factors, such as personality traits, coping skills, experience, social support, 
and the nature of the stressors or challenges faced. It allows individuals to navigate various life situations, 
including evacuations, other disasters, or personal adversities, with greater resilience and effectiveness. 
==PAGE:29==
 
 
28 
However, while some individuals may demonstrate adaptive capabilities, others may struggle. Understanding 
of the unique challenges in cognitive flexibility, sensory processing, and social communication may make 
increase understanding and support for psychological adaptation in promoting evacuation skills in individuals 
with ASD.  
 
Summing up the previous chapter and building on the understanding of ASD as it is seen now and the 
psychological processes that individuals with ASD experience during evacuations, such as cognitive appraisal, 
emotion regulation, and adaptation, it becomes clear that a thorough investigation is needed to address 
these challenges.  
 
The next chapter goes deeper into an analysis of a group of individuals with ASD, to investigate further how 
it might be possible to understand their needs in a more personalized way. Exploring how these individuals 
understand and adapt to high-stress situations requires not only examining their personal subjective 
experiences but also quantifying the impact of various stressors on their ability to cope and make decisions. 
By focusing on both the qualitative aspects-such as how individuals with ASD emotionally and cognitively 
adapt to emergency scenarios – and the quantitative data, a more comprehensive understanding of their 
unique needs during evacuations.  This approach enables us to more fully capture the intricate balance 
between psychological adaptation and practical response, guiding the next steps in formulating tailored 
interventions.  
 
3. Methodology  
It is important to tailor methodologies to effectively capture the complexities of ASD during emergency 
evacuations. This chapter outlines a mixed-methods approach, combining qualitative depth with quantitative 
width to explore the multifaceted experiences and responses of individuals with ASD. The integration of semi-
structured interviews and systematic data collection methods allows for a more comprehensive analysis of 
both personal experiences and broader patterns, ensuring a thorough examination of the unique challenges 
and adaptive strategies employed by individuals with ASD during imagined or real evacuations. This synergy 
is designed to give a more nuanced understanding that may be critical for developing targeted, effective 
interventions. 
 
==PAGE:30==
 
 
29 
3.1 Research Design and Approach (Qualitative) and Data Collection Methods (Semi-structured Interviews) 
To effectively address the unique challenges faced by individuals with ASD during evacuations, it is important 
to utilize a research approach that captures both the depth of their experiences and the measurable outcome 
of interventions. Therefore, this study employs a mixed methods approach, combining qualitative insights 
with qualitative data. The objective is to gain a deeper understanding of the lived experience of individuals 
with ASD and their understanding of evacuation. The qualitative approach will allow for an exploration of 
complexities and nuances of their experience and the quantitative approach may help towards a more 
generalized understanding of a larger population. 
 
Qualitative methods, focuses on non-numerical data such as opinions, feelings, behaviors, and attitudes. 
Common techniques include interviews, focus groups and observations, among others. The qualitative aspect 
helps explore personal accounts, emotional responses, and coping mechanisms, providing a nuanced 
understanding of how individuals with ASD perceive and maybe react to evacuation scenarios. It often 
involves open-ended questions to encourage participants to express their thoughts and opinions in their own 
words. Analysis is typically subjective and interpretive (Lewis, 2015) .  
 
On the other hand, the quantitative component allows for the collection of statistical data, such as number 
of previous trainings with evacuations, confidence levels, and preferred way of information, which could offer 
a measurable foundation for assessing evacuation as is. To ensure that the participants can answer the 
questionnaire, some more simple questions have also been included. Where a more structured approach 
with closed-ended questions, with predetermined response options available allowing for easier 
quantification and analysis (more of a Likert scale type)(Harwell, 2011) .  
 
Together, these methods offer a comprehensive view, ensuring that the emotional, cognitive, and practical 
aspects of evacuation are thoroughly examined. This method allows us to bridge the gap between subjective 
experiences and objective measures, offering a well-rounded analysis of evacuation challenges and potential 
solutions for individuals with ASD. See Appendix 1. 
 
3.2 Participant Recruitment and Selection Criteria  
To recruit participants for this study a multi-pronged approach was used. Several board schools for autists 
where contacted and psychologists who worked with autists were contacted. Also, the Autismforening and 
other online communities were contacted to see if they would/could facilitate recruitment efforts and reach 
==PAGE:31==
 
 
30 
out to their community for support of the project. In the end it proved quite difficult and in general schools 
were afraid to include their pupils. 
 
Inclusion criteria for participation include: 
• 
Formal diagnosis of autism spectrum disorder by qualified professional. 
• 
Age between 14-35 years old. 
• 
Ability to provide informed consent, in person or by proxy. 
• 
Ability to participate in semi-structured interview, performed by a licensed psychologist. 
 
Exclusion criteria for participants include: 
• 
Cognitive disability or communication disability that significantly hinders participation in interview. 
• 
Non-verbal 
• 
Mental health condition that impacts the reliability of the interview response 
 
The aim was to recruit a sample size of 5-8 participants. However, the final sample size was determined by 
availability and was 6.  
 
3.3 Ethical Codex 
Well-being and safety of participants are paramount in the field of research and even more so when working 
with persons with autism. All recruitment practices and interviews will adhere to The Danish Code of Conduct 
for Research Integrity and research guidelines, ensuring informed consent and participant confidentiality 
(The Danish Code of Conduct for Research Integrity — English, 2024) . 
 
3.3.1 Participant Well-being 
All participants who can read will be provided with an information sheet explaining the purpose of the study 
and for those who cannot read it will be read aloud. If clients have guardians, they will be informed and may 
give consent. Participants will have the opportunity to ask questions and if any doubts they will have time to 
decide before participating. All participants (or guardians) will be free to withdraw from the study at any 
point and may withdraw their data from the analysis. If only partial data is available this will also be used, 
and context will be made available for the project. 
Referral resources will be made available to the participants if they require additional support or experience 
distress during the interview process. One participant had additional support, but the support individual did 
not interfere in the interview. 
==PAGE:32==
 
 
31 
 
3.3.2 Informed Consent 
A written informed consent form was obtained from each participant before the interview began. The form 
will (Appendix 2): 
• 
Clearly explain the study objectives and procedures.  
• 
Outline the participant’s rights and responsibilities, including the right to withdraw. 
• 
Guarantee confidentiality of all data collected. 
• 
Participants may receive a copy of the signed consent form for their records if they wish. 
 
3.3.3 Data Confidentiality 
• 
All data collected during interviews will be treated with confidentiality. 
• 
Participants was be assigned numbers during data analysis and reporting stages to protect identities. 
• 
Interview recording and transcripts will be stores securely and access will be to authorized 
researchers only. 
• 
Data will be destroyed or anonymized beyond recognition after 1 year. 
 
The study was in accordance with National Ethical Guidelines (Nationalt Center for Etik), and the researcher 
have previously received training in ethical research practice to ensure responsible conduct during and 
after the study. Also, a research ethics statement has been made before the study was initiated (Appendix 
4). 
 
3.4 Data Analysis Techniques (Thematic Analysis) 
The data analysis employed to analyze the qualitative data collected from the semi structured interviews was 
thematic analyzed, to identify recurring themes and patterns within the data, with the hope of providing 
insight into participants experiences and perspectives (Harwell, 2011). The process has been adapted to fit 
the study and may not involve all processes in a standard thematic analysis. 
 
Thematic analysis process: 
• 
Familiarization: The researcher recorded, thoroughly transcribed and read all interviews to gain a 
comprehensive understanding of the data and immerse herself in the participants narrative. 
• 
Initial thematic: The researcher coded data to capture key concepts, experiences, and emotions 
expressed by the participants. 
• 
Themes: Themes that represent recurring data across the data will be brought up and discussed. 
==PAGE:33==
 
 
32 
 
For the quantitative analysis a different set of methods was used. Initially the data was organised in a clear 
and consistent format using an excel spreadsheet all data was be checked for errors, missing values, or 
inconsistencies. The data obtained from the spreadsheet was summarized and important descriptive 
statistics was explored (Harwell, 2011). If any relationships between variables is seen, they will be 
summarized, and key findings will be described, and conclusions will be drawn on the basis of literature. 
  
By following these procedures, the aim is to conduct a reliable thematic and quantitative analysis, gaining 
insight from participants interviews. 
 
4 Findings & Discussion 
In this chapter the nuanced findings derived from the data, collected on how individuals with ASD navigate 
the complexities of evacuations, is presented. The investigation offers a multifaceted understanding of 
barriers, facilitators, and experiential realities these individuals face during such critical events. 
Through analysis of interview transcripts and survey data, this chapter presents the key themes that have 
emerged. This includes participants characteristics, cognitive functioning, sensory sensitivities, 
communication challenges and social dynamics, all of which plays a role in shaping how individuals with ASD 
experience evacuation. 
 
It is also explored what the implication of these findings might be, discussing how this is in relation to current 
practices and suggest alternatives for innovation, more effective emergency management strategies that are 
inclusive of neurodiverse populations. By analyzing the experiences of the participants, this chapter highlights 
unique challenges faced by individuals with ASD but also underscores the potential adaptive strategies that 
is relevant for their individual needs.  
 
Through this discussion it is a hope that this will contribute to the broader discourse on evacuation 
preparedness, advocating for approaches that enhance safety and accessibility, thereby fostering resilience 
among one of the more vulnerable segments of our society in evacuation situations.  
 
4.1 Overview of Participants' Characteristics  
As there are only 6 participants in this study, they will be described in a more general manner, so no 
identification of individuals is possible. 
==PAGE:34==
 
 
33 
Participants were between the age of 14 and 35. They had all been diagnosed with ASD in their childhood or 
their early teens. All but one was attending Krabbeshus, which is a day school specialized and dedicated to 
students with ASD, providing tailored educational environments that supports their unique learning needs. 
The school focuses on creating structured, supportive, and understanding settings that facilitate the personal 
and educational developments with its students. With specialized staff and customized teaching methods, 
Krabbeshus Heldagsskole aims to enhance each student’s ability to function and thrive both academically 
and socially. The school’s approach emphasizes the importance of individualized plans and close 
collaboration between teachers, therapists, and families to ensure a holistic educational experience. 
Krabbeshus Heldagsskole represents critical resource in the community, offering a space where students with 
ASD can gain skills and confidence.  
The philosophy and environment of Krabbeshus Heldagsskole serves as an important case study in 
understanding how it is possible to reduce anxiety and enhance comprehension through communication and 
predictable routines. This is consistent with the notion that individuals with ASD are better able to thrive and 
be more competent in environments that meet their specific needs 
 
4.2 Themes and Patterns Emerging from the Data 
This analysis explores the cognitive and sensory experiences of six individuals with ASD, unveiling themes in 
their interactions with the environment and social settings. The interviews reveal important insight into 
cognitive functioning, patterns in sensory sensitivities, communication skills, social understanding and 
previous experiences with emergencies. These were explored to better understand their daily and emergency 
response behaviors. The complex interplay between cognitive functioning, social understanding, and 
adaptive behaviors in individuals with ASD is a critical area of research. 
 
The narratives reveal not only the varied intellectual abilities assessed potentially through the WISC 
(Wechsler Intelligence Scale for Children 6-16), but also the diverse ways participants perceive and react to 
sensory stimuli and social cues. Key findings highlight how these individuals navigate practical challenges 
such as evacuations and adapt to their environments, reflecting on their own abilities and coping strategies 
in both routine and stress-inducing situations. The findings from this study provide insight into the diverse 
cognitive abilities and adaptive challenges faced by individuals with ASD, particularly in the context of 
emergencies such as evacuations. This analysis aims to enhance our understanding of the autistic experience 
and will be used to further discuss the previous theoretical framework and models of psychological 
adaptation.  
 
==PAGE:35==
 
 
34 
4.2.1 Cognitive Functioning 
The overlap between ASD and Intellectual Disability (ID) highlighted by (Matson et al., 2009) emphasizes that 
approximately 40% of individuals with ASD also meet the criterial for intellectually disability. This statistic 
underscores the necessity for interventions that are cognizant of the dual impact of ASD and ID on cognitive 
and adaptive functioning. The variability in intellectual ability among individuals with ASD, as evident of the 
WISC assessment mentioned, ranging from low IQ indicative of significant cognitive challenges to high IQ, 
aligns with the literature the emphasizes a spectrum of cognitive abilities with ASD populations (Charman et 
al., 2011) . 
 
Understanding this overlap is important for interventions that address the complex spectrum of cognitive, 
social, and adaptive challenges presented by both conditions (Matson et al., 2009). Similarly cognitive 
functioning may influence how individuals with ASD process information and make decisions during an 
evacuation or how they asses their own abilities, which may affect behavior during evacuation.  
The study included 6 males of varied intellectual abilities. It was not clear what test had been done to, but 
questions asked by the interviewer and answered by the participants, would indicate it was a WISC-test. The 
WISC test is used to test cognitive ability, cognitive function, and whether a child is gifted. The test is normally 
administered by a trained psychologist. 
 
Two of the participants had specific IQ scores and 4 participants had been assessed to be within normal range 
of 90-110. One of the participants had an IQ score of 66 which would constitute a lower grade of mental 
retardation and he had a person present to assist during the interview, although not because of the mental 
retardation, but due to behavior. The other participant who had a specific IQ score, had an IQ of 135 which 
would constitute a high IQ.  
 
In the interviews it is evident that the cognitive functioning varies widely, but some insights have been 
extracted. Participants demonstrated a surprising level of self-awareness regarding their abilities to navigate 
hypothetical emergency scenarios, applying logical thinking to evacuation processes. As they were able to 
express their thought processes and considerations for safety in hypothetical scenarios. This suggest that, 
despite cognitive challenges, individuals with ASD can exhibit resilience and adaptability in structured tasks. 
However, the difference in cognitive functioning underscores the need for training to ensure knowledge of 
evacuation and tailored emergency responses, as lower cognitive function may affect one’s ability to process 
complex evacuation procedures. The findings align with Pellicano’s (2012) research, which suggest that 
cognitive function in ASD is highly contextual. 
==PAGE:36==
 
 
35 
In terms of adaptability, participants demonstrate varying degrees of stress management and coping 
mechanisms in response to sudden changes, such as emergency evacuation drills. Some express a preference 
for unplanned drills, suggesting they feel more confident handling real-life scenarios without warning, 
indicating resilience and ability to function under stress. Even if this might be self-overestimation.  
 
Social understanding and interactions was also a part of the interview, with participants describing 
experiences of social isolation or difficulties, such as bullying due to their diagnosis. This points to a level of 
social and emotional understanding concerning their own experiences and possibly the impact of those 
experiences on their cognitive and social functioning. 
 
Overall, while a direct assessment of cognitive functioning related to IQ or specific cognitive testing was not 
possible, the interviews provide a qualitative insight into how these individuals imagine navigating various 
social and practical scenarios.  
 
4.2.2 Sensory Sensitivities 
All participants reported varying degrees of sensory sensitivity, particularly to noise, lights, and crowds. For 
example, one participant was more sensitive to low-frequent humming sounds, while another struggled with 
flickering lights. Such sensitivities can significantly impact behavior in emergency situations, where alarms 
and chaotic environments are common.  
 
Although some also reported that they, if there was a need for an alarm, that it would be preferable if the 
alarm would shut down after a period and a voice could take over. Some preferred a voice alarm only. One 
participant was more sensitive to lower humming sounds, rather that high loud noises. 
Sensitivity to lights were only reported from one participant and that was especially flickering light, as 
described by driving past trees with the sun in the background. 
 
Several of the participants described challenges with crowds, especially if they were of unknown people. The 
participants from Krabbeshus, were generally quite good with smaller crowds, such as classrooms with more 
people. Although they also described needing time to themselves, which was made possible with individual 
rooms as well as classrooms.
