The participants were generally not comfortable with touch, but when asked how they might help their 
friends evacuate, more than one described that they would gently put a hand on the shoulder of the friend 
==PAGE:37==
 
 
36 
and tell them where to go. They were quite confident, that it would be possible to assist their friends in 
evacuation situations. 
 
Each individual response did vary significantly, highlighting the need for more personalized approaches in 
support and intervention strategies. Although the participants expressed preference for sensory-friendly 
evacuation protocols, such as voice alarms over loud sirens. This highlights the importance of designing 
emergency responses that account for sensory sensitivities, as overwhelming stimuli can lead to increased 
stress and confusion. This aligns with Green et al. (2010), who emphasize the behavioral impact of sensory 
overload in individuals with ASD. 
 
4.2.3 Communication Skills 
Communication challenges were evident across participants and there is no doubt that communication skills 
affect how individuals understand and follow instructions and how they express their needs and concerns 
before and during crisis. This lack of communication skills may influence the ability to evacuate and their 
perception of the event afterwards, which can make a difference if they have had evacuation experiences 
before and could also have an effect afterwards (Rumball et al., 2020). Some participants mention how 
sensory inputs, like loud sounds or bright lights, can affect their communication and interaction. This 
sensitivity may influence their engagement in a conversation as they may hesitate to discuss uncomfortable 
experiences or their overall communicative behavior, if they have had negative or positive experiences they 
may want to share. 
 
It was seen that there was a trend towards more direct form of communication in the interviews, often 
focusing on concrete or literal interpretations of questions and situations. This directness sometimes 
manifested the need for explicit instructions or lack of implied understanding of social ques. Although this 
was not solely the fault of the participant, but also the interviewer could at time had chosen explanations 
that would be more appropriate for the autistic mind. 
 
The participants were seen to vary in their ability to express complex ideas or emotions. Some could articulate 
their thoughts clearly, while others struggled to show preference for simpler or more direct forms of 
expressions. Such as given the choice of how to practice evacuation. When faced with hypothetical questions, 
participants often focus on practical solutions or express confusion or difficulty in engaging with hypothetical 
constructs. This can highlight challenges in abstract thinking, which is a common trait in individuals with ASD. 
==PAGE:38==
 
 
37 
Several participants express a preference for routine or clear, specific instructions. This indicates reliance on 
predictable patterns and concrete guidelines in social and operational contexts, which helps in reducing 
ambiguity and anxiety. These patterns may be established with training, not only in emergency situations, 
but in common situations, such as shopping or other social activities. 
 
Given the reliance on clear, literal communication, it is essential to tailor evacuation procedures to provide 
unambiguous instructions. Practice, visual aids and pre-recorded voice messages could be effective in 
enhancing comprehension for this population, reducing the risk of miscommunication during critical 
moments. 
 
4.2.4 Social Understanding 
Some of the participants discussed difficulties in recognizing and responding to social cues, which is a 
common and expected characteristic in individuals with ASD. For instance, one of the participants were asked 
if he could recognize his minders mood and if he was happy or mad. He replied: “He is always mad” When 
asked if he could tell if the interviewer was happy or mad, he just replied: “No!”. These difficulties in 
recognizing the mind of others based on social cues, highlight the need for more explicit communication. 
 
As several of the participants also told the interviewer, they had been subjected to bullying, because of their 
diagnosis. The social challenges and experiences of bullying reported in the interviews are consistent with 
the broader literature that documents the social vulnerabilities of individuals with ASD (Cappadocia et al., 
2012) . These experiences not only affect cognitive and emotional development but also influence the 
efficacy of interventions aimed at improving adaptive responses in community settings, including schools and 
public places. 
 
These negative past experiences may impact their social comfort and interactions. This may cause them to 
withdraw from social interaction altogether and was described by the participants as making school 
environments particularly challenging. Several participants touched on experiences of social 
misunderstandings, which could lead to feelings of isolation or being misunderstood by others. This could 
reflect the broader difficulties individuals with ASD might have in social interpretive processes. These were 
especially in relation to transitions to new educational settings, of which all reported, where changes often 
involved adjusting to new social norms and making new friends. 
Even so, one of the participants reflected on the positive aspect of friendships even in difficult school 
environments, suggesting a nuanced view of social relations where positive interactions coexists with 
==PAGE:39==
 
 
38 
challenges. Which would also indicate that strong bonds, targeted support and interventions, could mitigate 
the difficulty of understanding social cues in general and more specific with regards to challenging situations 
such as evacuations. 
 
The challenges in reading social cues may contribute to social isolation, which several participants 
experienced in their school environments. These experiences of isolation and bullying highlight the need for 
inclusive strategies that foster social connection and understanding in emergency planning. As baron-Cohen 
et al. (198) suggest, explicit communication of social expectations may help mitigate misunderstandings and 
promote better responses during evacuations.  Social understanding is in this way important and influences 
how individuals with ASD perceive and react to the behavior of others around them during evacuations. 
 
4.2.5 Evacuation and Emergency Preparedness 
The ability of individuals with ASD to apply logical thinking to evacuation processes, despite varied intellectual 
abilities, suggests that cognitive function in ASD is highly contextual and task dependent (Pellicano, 2012) 
This supports the notion that while individual with ASD may show challenges in certain cognitive domains, 
they can exhibit competence in specific structures tasks the require logical problem-solving. However, 
literature suggests that the actual performance in real-world chaotic situations, such as during an emergency 
evacuation, may differ significantly due to increased demands on sensory processing and executive 
functioning(Ashburner et al., n.d.). 
 
Previous experience with trauma can shape future responses to similar situations. Positive or negative past 
experiences can either enhance preparedness or increase anxiety and hesitation during future evacuations 
(Kerns et al., 2015). Several of the participants discussed their experience in scenarios that could potentially 
be perceived as stressful or traumatic, particularly in relation to their sensitivity to confrontational situations. 
One participant mentioned distress during a sports activity, highlighting the common challenge of individuals 
with ASD who may have problem interpreting group activities or competitive environments.  
 
Another participant recounted an experience with a dog that led to a stressful situation. The participant 
described walking home from a bus stop, when a dog began to chase him. This unexpected encounter caused 
the participant to experiencing intense fear as he raced home. This situation escalated the participant’s stress 
levels significantly, highlighting the vulnerability in unexpected and uncontrolled environments. 
In one interview, a participant recounted an event involving the discovery of a deceased person (by third 
party) on campus. This occurred in the context of a school setting, adding a layer of complexity and emotional 
==PAGE:40==
 
 
39 
weight to the incident. The participant detailed that there were initial concerns about a possible gas leak, 
which compounded the anxiety and uncertainty of the situation. It was later revealed that it was suicide. The 
story was collaborated by the school principal. 
 
Despite the clarification, the presence of emergency services and the initial fear significantly impacted the 
school community. Even despite of the initial fear, the participant later described the evacuation as non-
traumatic.  
 
Participants generally expressed a preference for unplanned drills, as they perceived them as better for their 
understanding of their own patterns of behavior. Several of the participants was very expressive about the 
need for evacuation drills that mimic real-life stress, potentially as a coping mechanism or a way to ensure 
preparedness in real emergencies. It might also be a sign of their lack of emergency drills, most of them could 
only remember emergency drills from another school, which was 3-4 years ago. This might also have been 
why they actively opted out of the opportunity for stress-free prepared events, when presented to them as 
options. A unprepared evacuation drill may be more effective training for real-life scenarios, which is 
reflected in the findings by Donnellan (2006), which also suggest that exposure to realistic stressors can 
enhance adaptability and preparedness in individual with ASD. However, this approach must be balanced 
with the potential for heightened stress and anxiety, which are common in ASD and can adversely affect 
overall functioning during actual emergencies (S. Green et al., 2010). Although this might not be specific for 
individuals with ASD, but also more general.  
 
The insights from previous experiences with evacuations, suggest that such experiences, particularly those 
that involve unexpected or high-pressure situations, could significantly impact stress response and coping 
strategies. Stress response and coping strategies should be explored further to understand how such 
experiences shape individual coping mechanisms and daily interactions, especially in structured 
environments like educational settings, where unexpected changes may be more stressful. Although the 
most important lesson might be that they are not necessarily traumatized by the events or later in be 
confronted and talking about these previous stressful events.  
 
The findings from the interviews underscores the necessity for a differentiated approach in designing 
emergency response interventions for individuals with ASD. Future research should continue to explore the 
efficacy of realistic drill scenarios versus controlled training environments, and how these approaches impact 
stress response and overall preparedness of individuals with ASD. Additionally, longitudinal studies could 
==PAGE:41==
 
 
40 
provide deeper insights into how repeated exposure to structured emergency drills influence adaptive 
behaviors and stress resilience over time. 
 
4.3 Facilitators and Strategies for Enhancing Psychological Adaptation and Evacuation 
Outcomes 
This part of the analysis turns towards the positive, exploring the facilitators that may support individuals 
with ASD during evacuations and the strategies that may enhance their psychological adaptation and safety. 
The importance of individual needs in the context of evacuation is discussed. This includes examining the 
efficacy of specific interventions such as structured environments, visual aids, and the use of technology to 
aid communication during stressful situations. Additionally, the broader implication of these facilitators is 
explored, considering how they may be integrated into evacuation protocols, to accommodate and support 
neurodiverse populations in evacuation. 
 
Hopefully the discussion will provide actionable insights for emergency planners, educators, caregivers, and 
policymakers to develop more inclusive evacuation strategies. This approach should ensure that the safety 
measures not only meet the needs of those with ASD but also enhance their ability to navigate challenging 
evacuations with greater independence and confidence. 
 
4.3.1 Medical and Social Models in Facilitating Evacuation Strategies for Individuals with ASD 
The way ASD is conceptualized significantly influences interventions for individuals with ASD and how 
evacuation strategies are approached. By examining both the medical and social model of disability, it may 
be better understood how to create inclusive and adaptive evacuation strategies that understands and 
considers the unique needs of individuals on the spectrum. 
 
The medical model of disability views ASD a primarily as a neurological or cognitive disorder that affects an 
individual’s ability to process sensory information, communicate, and adapt to change. From this perspective, 
interventions focus on managing symptoms and improving functioning through individualized treatments 
such as behavioral therapy, sensory integration, and medication (Bishop, 2014). 
 
In the context of evacuation strategies for individuals with ASD, the medical model would likely emphasize, 
that individuals with ASD often experience sensory overload in emergency situations due to loud alarms, 
flashing lights, or chaotic environments. Interventions may include sensory aids like noise cancelling 
headphones, dimmable lighting or personalized evacuation alarms with softer tones (Martin & Wilkins, 
==PAGE:42==
 
 
41 
2021). Cognitive behavioral interventions can help individuals with ASD recognize and manage their 
emotional response, by practicing decision-making in controlled environments, such as emergency drills, 
individuals can better cope with the flight-fight-freeze response The use of assistive devices such as 
communication board or mobile applications that guide the individuals through step-by-step evacuation 
procedures could also be effective in reducing stress and confusion during emergencies (Alcantud et al., 
2016). 
 
As previously described the medical model provides valuable insights into individual-focused interventions, 
it can be limiting as it is often centered on “fixing” the individual rather that adjusting the environment to 
accommodate their needs. This is where the social model of disability offers a more inclusive and adaptive 
approach. 
 
The social model is more about designing inclusive environments for evacuations. In evacuations scenarios, 
this model promotes designing evacuation systems and environments that support the specific needs of the 
individual, with ASD. Emergency evacuation infrastructure should be designed to reduce sensory overload. 
This could include visual pathways with clear (and exclusive) signage, calm zones where individuals with ASD 
could regroup during evacuations, similar to safe zones for wheelchairs as it is seen today, but with a focus 
on sensory-friendly environments with muted lighting and reduced noise levels. By minimizing overwhelming 
stimuli, these spaces could help prevent anxiety or panic during evacuations. 
  
Communication have traditionally been a loud sound but have become more and more specific. Stimm- 
friendly alarms (alarms designed to be less intrusive or triggering for individuals sensitive to noise) could also 
be integrated on a more personal level, as it could allow individuals with ASD to react more calmly in contrast 
to the overwhelming sound of standard loud alarms (Leffel, 2004). 
 
The social model also advocates for communication systems that are universally accessible. Wayfinding 
systems with clear, non-verbal, simple pictograms and visual aids, rather than complex verbal instructions, 
can also assist in navigating evacuation routes more easily, reduce cognitive overload and increase 
comprehension (D. Nunes et al., 2007; Velasco et al., 2012).  
 
Practice and routine are important for individuals with ASD, who often rely on predictability. Pre-emptive 
evacuation drills tailored specifically for them may help to desensitize them to the stimuli associated with 
evacuations. These drills could include walkthroughs of the evacuation process with familiar caregivers, visual 
==PAGE:43==
 
 
42 
aids, and roleplaying exercises that allow individuals to practice the expected behavior in a low-pressure 
graded setting (Wilczynski et al., 2007).  The practice could be developed with regards for individual sensory 
or cognitive needs. Such as, individuals who are sensitive to touch or proximity to others may need alternative 
routes or designated safe spaces (Begeer et al., 2011). 
 
The social model would also include community and social support. Community-based evacuation strategies 
should include training for first responders and community members on how to interact with and support 
individuals with ASD during evacuations. This may involve recognizing signs of sensory overload or stress, 
using clear, direct communication, and ensuring individuals have aces to their sensory tools or 
communication devices (Blackwell & Rossetti, 2014). The social models also advocate the peer support 
system, where individuals with ASD are paired with trusted peers or caregivers who understand their specific 
needs and can assist in evacuation scenarios (Ozbay et al., 2007). 
 
The neurodiversity perspective, which emphasizes the value of cognitive differences, aligns with the social 
model by promoting strategies that honor the unique needs and strengths of individuals with ASD rather 
than focusing solely on deficits (Armstrong, 2015). By adopting both the medical and the social model 
approaches, it may be possible to create more comprehensive and inclusive evacuation strategies that 
enhances psychological adaptation for individuals with ASD. This includes both personal interventions and 
systemic changes, ultimately fostering environments where individuals with ASD can feel safe, supported, 
and empowered to respond efficiently in emergency situations. But most important of all, is that they 
practice. So even if none of the individual requirements are at hand, they should, like all others, practice 
evacuation.  
 
4.3.2 Understanding Appraisal and Reappraisal in Evacuation Strategies for Individuals with ASD 
For individuals with ASD, the perception and cognitive appraisal of emergency situations like evacuations 
may be challenging. In understanding evacuation responses for individuals with ASD, it is important to 
examine how cognitive and emotional processes interact with atypical responses. This might influence both 
perception of emergency situations, but also highlight areas where evacuation strategies can be adapted to 
better support individuals with ASD. 
 
Research have shown that individuals with ASD might have difficulty with the rapid reassessment required 
when condition change unexpectedly, which is often the case in evacuations. This challenge is linked to the 
atypical neural processing found in ASD, particularly in the areas involved in decision making and emotional 
==PAGE:44==
 
 
43 
regulation (Modinos et al., 2010; Solomon, Ozonoff, et al., 2008). Here it becomes important to incorporate 
practice of evacuation, increase the knowledge of what is expected of the individuals when an evacuation is 
needed. This would reduce the need for decision-making and emotional regulation. 
 
Resistance to change or rigidity as described previously, is a well-documented aspect of ASD and would 
significantly impact the effectiveness of evacuation procedures. Several of the participants expressed 
problems with rigidity and it was also reported that if sameness or routines were changed, aggression was 
often one of the results. One of the participants described that even if it was familiar people, such as family, 
who did things that was not planned or previously approved by the participant. It could make him aggressive. 
This is where regular and predictable evacuation drills (that may or may not be specifically designed) for 
individuals with ASD may assist in preparing and familiarizing the individual with evacuation. This may help 
reduce any anxiety associated with unexpected change (Petrolini et al., 2023). This is also a possibility to 
gradually desensitize to the elements of the evacuation in a clear and structured format. Visual aids to reduce 
perception of unpredictability and for better support of the required actions during evacuations (Mesibov et 
al., 2005). 
 
Even if several of the participants expressed that they would prefer an unplanned evacuation, to be able to 
experience and learn to handle the stress and panic they imagined they would feel, this would not be 
recommended. As the participants may not be fully aware of how it would feel, as they have only scarcely 
tried evacuation drills. One participant was asked if he had ever experienced panic and he told the story of 
him being chased by a dog and how much it had and still affected him, several years after the incident. There 
seemed to be a level of self-overestimation in almost all the participants. Where they gave the impression 
that they would be able to handle almost all the challenges that was put before them. An example was one 
of the participants, who when asked what he would do if ta fire blocked his exit door. He proceeded to tell 
that he would jump out of his window, be then suddenly remembered there was a hedge there. Which threw 
him of the idea. Which it probably would not have done for most people, when the choice was between fire 
and a hedge. 
 
Gradual desensitization might be preferable, to strictly unplanned evacuation drills. Although rigidity and 
preferences for routine can create challenges during unpredictable situations like evacuations, these traits 
can be leveraged through routine-based evacuation drills. Repetition and familiarity reduce anxiety, allowing 
individuals with ASD to perform better on actual emergencies (South et al., 2007). In conjunction with 
==PAGE:45==
 
 
44 
personalized safe spaces or evacuation routes it may ease the transition from routine to emergency 
situations.   
 
Deficits in theory of mind can hinder an individual’s ability to understand others in general, as seen in 
individuals with ASD, but also more specifically why others may be evacuating or follow group behavior (Frith 
& Frith, 2003). For example, if a person appraisees a facial expression as fear or despair they might respond 
with fear themselves, if they appraise it as a determination, they might follow the person as they trust them. 
This ability to appraise the intentions or emotions of behind social cues accurately is critical for effective 
social communication (Lazarus, 1993). Frequent negative appraisals in social setting such as the ones seen by 
the participants in the study, where they experience bullying, conflicts and complex relationships may lead 
to further misunderstanding in social cues. Therefore, they might be affected in a more negative way which 
may influence quality and stability of relationships (Gross, 2015). It has been seen that positive and negative 
outcomes of past interactions can shape how new situations are appraised, which in turn influence future 
social behavior(A Bandura, 1978).  This was seen in the participants as one of them had positive interactions 
not in school, but with his friend at the scouts. He was very trusting with them, but at the same time he 
reported that he had little trust with his school “friends”.  He had training, with the scouts, since childhood 
and had many interesting stories of camaraderie and excursions with them. At the same time, he was at the 
school because he was very outwardly aggressive, when previous teachers had disciplined him. 
 
Understanding the connection between cognitive appraisal and social communication may help develop 
interventions and strategies to improve social skills as seen in some of the participants. Training such as 
targeted social skills training may help in improving perspective-taking and follow collective actions. Non-
verbal cues such as group movement or visual leadership by responders may guide individuals without relying 
on complex verbal instructions (Castelli et al., 2002). Similarly, when asked how others or the participants 
would like to be helped when an evacuation might be necessary, they showed a very high level of theory of 
mind. The described how it would be less intrusive if someone would put a hand on their shoulder (or they 
would put a hand on their friend’s shoulder) and lead them out. This showed, even if they had said they did 
not prefer physical contact, that showing someone they way in case of emergency would be okay.  That this 
might give a sense of security for themselves as well as others. 
 
The above is a good example of how cognitive appraisal, involves changing one’s emotional response by 
altering the interpretation of a situation (Hendricks & Buchanan, 2016). This can be a beneficial approach 
during evacuations, as it helps manage fear, resistance, stress, and anxiety. However, research shows that 
==PAGE:46==
 
 
45 
individuals with ASD may struggle with spontaneous reappraisal due to impaired emotional regulation. Some 
individuals with ASD exhibit atypical activation of the prefrontal cortex, which may affect their ability to 
regulate emotions and engage in reappraisal (Dorit Ben Shalom, 2009). These difficulties underscore the need 
for interventions strategies that focuses on tailored sensory management and a more general emotional 
regulation training for individuals with ASD, especially in emergency situations (Begeer et al., 2011). To 
enhance cognitive reappraisal during evacuations, individuals with ASD may benefit from training and 
practice, as it takes time and consistent practice to master evacuation on a conscious level. Although a 
powerful tool, reappraisal in high-stress situations like evacuation can be challenging to generate and apply 
alternative interpretations. Unsuccessful attempts may increase anxiety. However, it can also facilitate 
quicker, more effective action if applied appropriately, helping individuals react constructively and efficiently 
during emergency situations. 
 
Given these challenges, several strategies may be employed to facilitate better evacuation. Cognitive 
behavioral training with structured simulation exercises can help ASD individuals practice reframing stressful 
situations like alarms or crowds, enabling a more constructive response (Modinos et al., 2010).   
Implementing routine evacuation drills specifically tailored for individuals with ASD. These drills should 
gradually expose to stressors and include frequent repetition to improve their capacity for managing 
emotional responses during evacuations and the stimuli involved in evacuations (e.g., sirens, flashing lights, 
crowds) in a controlled manner to reduce anxiety during actual evacuations (Cuijpers, 2019) . 
 
It has also been suggested that, if possible, maintaining a routine or predictable environment during 
evacuations. For instance, it could be preferrable to allow individuals with ASD to carry personal items that 
provide comfort or using headphones to block out upsetting noises that might make the environment seem 
more controlled and less threatening (Attwood, 2006). Similarly in the interviews participants were asked 
about who made them feel comfortable or who they would like to help them during evacuations and all most 
all for the participant would mention a name of a trusted teacher (some from previous schools). A teacher 
they would have known for years. This is in line with previous research that have shown that caregivers and 
family members can play a critical role in helping individuals with ASD during evacuations by providing 
reassurance and guiding them through difficult processes. Their familiar presence can be significant source 
of comfort and stability (Henry & Myles, 2007). 
 
In summary, a more inclusive evacuation strategy not only enhances safety, but also promotes independence 
and confidence in high-stress situations, as individuals are empowered. Routine based drills provides 
==PAGE:47==
 
 
46 
predictability, reducing anxiety and improving the ability to reappraise difficult situations. Together with a 
more supportive approach and environment it may enable smoother, more constructive responses that 
benefit both individuals and emergency teams.  
 
4.3.3 The Two Path-Way model in Evacuation Strategies for Individuals with ASD 
To enhance evacuation strategies for individuals with ASD, through the lens of the LeDoux (2014) Two path-
way model, it is important to consider the complete dual process, in understanding how both neurotypical 
individuals and those with ASD respond to high-stress events like evacuations. By differentiating, but 
balancing, between the automatic low road and the deliberate high road responses to stress and applying 
this understanding it may lead to better tailored evacuation protocols that accounts for the unique emotional 
and sensory processing challenges faced by individuals with ASD. 
 
As the low road is often triggered by sensory overload and instead of solely relying on the previous mentioned 
calm zones, it may be considered implementing multisensory modulation zones (Reynolds et al., 2011). These 
areas would feature adjustable lighting, sound control, and tactile options. These zones can help regulate 
sensory input on a more individualized level, calming automatic reactions while supporting focus on 
evacuation instructions. Creating these zones involves understanding and integrating various sensory inputs 
in a way that supports rather than overwhelms the individual. This approach will not only enhance the ability 
to cope with sensory challenges but also support overall well-being. Together with this approach it could be 
specifically relevant in areas implementing a “stay put” strategy as often seen in high-rise buildings. The Stay-
Put strategy, also known as the “shelter in place”, is a safety protocol used in various emergency situations, 
such as fires, natural disasters, or threats of violence. The strategy involves remaining in a safe area instead 
of evacuation. The goal is to minimize exposure to dangerous conditions outside by staying in location that 
provides protection until it is safe to leave or until emergency responders gives instructions to evacuate. Or 
in the case of individuals with ASD, minimize the chance of an incident where they are overwhelmed and 
may cause further danger while being evacuated. Although the effectiveness and safety of the Stay Put 
strategy depends on specific circumstances of the emergency, the building and the immediate environment. 
The strategy has been criticized after the Greenfell Tower fire and specific circumstances should be evaluated 
in each case (A. O. Arewa et al., 2021). 
 
Alongside sensory modifications, the introduction of predictive auditory or visual clues can signal upcoming 
changes in the environment, preparing the individual emotionally and physically. For instance, a gentle chime 
(such as Stimm friendly alarms), that gradually increases in volume as the person approaches the higher-
==PAGE:48==
 
 
47 
sensory area (like crowded space or noisy exit) can help desensitize them to what is coming, reducing anxiety 
before they encounter overwhelming stimuli (Leffel, 2004). This would engage the low road preparing for 
action. 
 
Engaging the high road involves facilitating the prefrontal cortex activation, which supports deliberate 
emotional regulation and decision making (Gross, 2015). Individuals with ASD may struggle to switch from 
the low road to the high road due to atypical activation patterns in the prefrontal cortex, thus requiring 
additional support to encourage cognitive engagement. One novel strategy to help practice cognitive 
reappraisal and emergency responses could be Augmented Reality (AR) technology. AR can simulate 
emergency situations, allowing users to interact with their environment and practice calm, controlled 
responses in a safe setting. This method provides real-time feedback, enhancing their cognitive flexibility and 
ability to reappraise stressful situations (Modinos et al., 2010). Even if AR was not the preferred choice of the 
participants in his study, when ask about their preferred way of practicing evacuation, it should not be 
eliminated. As was seen there was a tendency to self-overestimate among participants and the AR could give 
a slower introduction into the world of evacuation and lower the risk of indecorous or inappropriate 
activation of the prefrontal cortex. 
 
Introducing behavioral anchoring techniques, such as pairing specific physical movements with calming 
responses, can also engage the high road. For instance, during evacuation training, individuals can be taught 
to take three deep breaths each time they reach a designated safety point (a sign or a door). This practice 
creates an automatic link between movement and emotional regulation, enabling quicker access to cognitive 
control during actual evacuations(Anderson & Farb, 2018). It can also be incorporated into daily practice for 
example if entering a room with crowds or places that in other ways may feel overwhelming. 
 
With the introduction of smart phones and clocks, a personalized digital guide, who could be integrated into 
a digital wearable device, could be developed to guide individuals during evacuations. This guide would offer 
step-by-step visual instructions combined with audio cues to help regulate the emotional responses. The 
guide could even detect stress levels via biosensors and adjust instructions in real-time, providing simpler 
steps or additional guidance when stress levels rise (C. Mazefsky et al., 2018). Together with noise cancelling 
headphones, it would reduce stress levels considerably. 
 
Emotional regulation and evacuation success may also be influenced by social support and cues. As has been 
described, individuals with ASD may have social interaction difficulties which may complicate their ability to 
==PAGE:49==
 
 
48 
interpret evacuation cues from others, further hindering their response to emergencies. However, specific 
social strategies can strengthen the response. Social role modeling can be incorporated into evacuation 
training. Neurotypical peers or trained professionals can demonstrate effective evacuation behavior in real-
world drills, allowing individuals with ASD to observe and mimic calm, structured responses. Repeated 
exposure to these models can help individuals internalize more controlled behavior during evacuations 
(Solomon, Ozonoff, et al., 2008). 
 
Similarly small, trusted peer networks specifically trained to assist individuals with ASD during evacuations 
can also be beneficial. These peers would be equipped with knowledge of the individuals’ sensory triggers 
and emotional needs, providing real-time support and guidance. The familiarity and reliability of this network 
may engage high road behavior and reduce the risk of panic through social cues and consistent behavior. 
 
To enhance evacuation strategies for individuals with ASD, integrating LeDoux’s Two-Pathway Model helps 
tailor emergency protocols by balancing instinctive and cognitive responses. Sensory modulation zones can 
alleviate sensory overloads, while predictive cues prepare individuals for intense environments. Augmented 
reality and behavioral anchoring techniques like linking physical movement to calming responses during 
training may foster cognitive control and flexibility. Implementing personal digital guides on wearable devices 
that can adapt instructions based on real-time stress levels, alongside social role modeling and peer support 
networks, can significantly aid individuals with ASD in making more informed, calm decisions during 
emergencies. This approach ensures a more tailored emergency preparedness that could enhance safety and 
compliance for individuals on the autism spectrum. 
 
4.3.4 Effective Decision-Making in Evacuation Strategies for Individuals with ASD 
As seen in the study autistic individuals often have unique cognitive profiles characterized by strengths in 
detail oriented, processing and challenges in social cognition and flexibility. Effective decision making in 
evacuation scenarios involves recognizing and leveraging these cognitive strengths while accommodation 
areas of difficulty. This may include providing clear, concrete information, breaking down complex tasks into 
smaller steps, and offering opportunities for rehearsal and repetition. 
 
Understanding how heuristics-cognitive shortcuts that facilitate quick decision-making operate, in such high 
stress environments as evacuation, is important for facilitating effective evacuation strategies that cater to 
the needs of individuals with ASD. Heuristics help reduce the cognitive load during decision-making by 
simplifying complex decisions into more manageable judgements (Kahneman & Tversky, 2018). While 
==PAGE:50==
 
 
49 
heuristics can expedite decision-making in emergencies, they often introduce biases. As seen in the 
interviews the participants there was a certain level of self-overestimation or overconfidence. 
 
Self-overestimation in normal functioning individuals is often influenced by cognitive biases such as the 
Dunning-Kruger Effect, where individuals with lower ability at a task overestimate their ability (Kruger et al., 
1999). This is particularly dangerous in emergency situations, where an accurate assessment of one’s abilities 
is crucial for safe evacuation. For individuals with ASD, who may fixate on specific details due to their 
cognitive profile, these biases can lead to challenges in making optimal decisions during evacuations. For 
instance, the anchoring bias may cause them to rely heavily on initial pieces of information they encounter, 
which might not always be the most relevant or safest option (Kahneman & Tversky, 2013). To mitigate the 
Dunning-Kruger effect and anchoring bias it is important to improve the skills of the participants, and thus 
increasing their competences, which has been seen to help recognize limitations in their abilities.  
 
Overconfidence in the context of risk perception and decision-making may alter both. When asked, none of 
the participants were in any doubt that they would be able to evacuate in the event of a fire. Even when 
informed about scenarios, that should have led to more questions or a slight hesitation. There was none.  
It is interesting to speculate where this overconfidence may come from. Social factors may play a role in 
evacuation behavior or thoughts of evacuation behavior. The presence of others who underestimate or 
underplay the risks related to fires may reinforce individual tendencies for self-overestimation, compounding 
risky behavior (Mileti & Sorensen, 1990).  Overprotective parenting behaviors have been shown to negatively 
influence development of coping strategies and adaptation skills (Arslan et al., 2023). This might mean that 
when parental or adult role models underplay risks, not only with regards to fires and evacuations, but they 
are also making the child overconfident in their abilities to act in emergency situations. Essentially creating a 
worse outcome. Even lack of evacuation training or too easy evacuation training might contribute to 
overconfidence.  
 
Overconfidence could lead to an underestimation of serious threat during an evacuation. This misjudgment 
may result in delayed evacuation, non-compliance with emergency protocols, or inappropriate actions during 
critical events (Weinstein, 1989). Research also indicates that overconfidence in one’s knowledge and 
abilities can lead to less precautionary behavior. In evacuation simulations, individuals exhibiting higher levels 
of overconfidence are more likely to ignore evacuation warnings or delay seeking safety (Paton, 2003). If this 
would apply to individuals with ASD it is not possible to say, but it is highly likely as it is a common human 
trait. 
==PAGE:51==
 
 
50 
 
Additionally, the availability heuristics, where individuals assess the probability of events based on the ease 
with which examples comes to mind, could lead to overestimating the safety of familiar but potentially 
dangerous routes during an evacuation. This was seen in several of the interviews where evacuation was very 
often seen very simplistically as “just getting out”. Another example from the interviews were: “I was told to 
use the stairs”. Question: “What if the stairs are not accessible?”. Answer: “I can use the stairs!”.  Where the 
person relies too much on initial information and anchoring their beliefs on initial information. Even 
availability heuristics come into play, as the exits in case of fire were always imagined as available, in almost 
all the participants.  
 
To address these challenges, evacuation strategies should focus on providing clear, consistent, and repeated 
information. Visual aids such as maps or pictorial instructions may be particularly effective, as they help 
counteract potential fixation on less critical details. These aids were also some of the information or aids that 
the interviewees asked for themselves. When repeatedly asked about difficult evacuations, the participants 
said they would like a map to show how to evacuate under different circumstances. Aids like these should 
highlight the most relevant information clearly and distinguish it from less critical details to avoid 
overwhelming the individual (Meadan et al., 2011). 
 
Training through simulation may also be on importance in preparing individuals with ASD for evacuation. 
Augmented Reality (AR) or similar technologies can create realistic scenarios that allow individuals to practice 
decision making in a controlled, repeatable environment. Although these technologies were not preferred 
by the participants, it might be because the interviewer did not fully explain how it could be used or what it 
entailed. These technologies may help individuals learn to prioritize information and make decisions under 
pressure, thereby reducing the adverse effects of heuristics (Blumenthal-Barby, 2016). Repeated exposure 
to simulated emergencies can help build familiarity of the process, reducing stress and the novelty of actual 
evacuations and helping in establishing a routine that can guide behaviour during real emergencies. 
 
As seen in the interviews, sensory sensitivities were prevalent with the participants as it is seen more 
generally in ASD, where individuals may be overwhelmed by stimuli, leading to sensory overload and 
impaired decision-making (DeBoth & Reynolds, 2017). In an evacuation scenario, this overload can be 
mitigated by designing environment-specific adaptations, such as lower intensity, non-flashing lights and 
providing noise-cancelling options or quieter evacuation routes. It has been seen that creating sensory 
friendly environments can reduce sensory overload in people with autism. By minimizing sensory stimuli, 
==PAGE:52==
 
 
51 
these environments can help individuals more comfortable and focused, even in new and challenging spaces 
such as theme parks (Leffel, 2004). Research have shown that sensory appropriate environment can improve 
attention, reduce anxiety and enhance overall functioning. This may include reducing noise levels, using 
sound-absorbing materials and providing noise-canceling headphones to reduce auditory stimuli. Which have 
been shown to reduce sympathetic nervous system reactivity (Pfeiffer et al., 2019).   
 
Reduced or controlled lighting, by the use of dimmable lights or sunglasses even take into consideration that 
some might respond to flickering lights (Martin & Wilkins, 2021; Nair et al., 2022). It could also include 
reducing visual stimuli in public areas, such as information boards, pictures in general and exchange them for 
meaningful visual clues. As attribution meaning to ambiguous visual stimuli, may prove a task that demands 
too much of the individual  (Klin, 2000). In the context of evacuation these information’s could help 
individuals designing evacuation or people working with autism, design areas where people with autism have 
a better chance of remaining calm and follow evacuation procedures more effectively, as previously detailed 
in the Stay Put Strategy. 
 
It has already been seen how pictograms are an effective way of communicating using visual support and 
how they may aid in understanding and following not only routines tasks, but also to systematic desensitize 
and familiarize with new environments (D. R. P. Nunes, 2008; Velasco et al., 2012). Visual support has also 
been shown to reduce anxiety and stress for individuals with autism by providing predictability, structure, 
and clarity in their environment (Gray, 2010) strengthening their ability to make sound decisions.  
 
Overall, pictograms may be a valuable tool for individuals with autism, providing visual support for 
commutation, learning, and daily living tasks. By leveraging the strengths of visual processing and 
representation, pictograms enhance accessibility, comprehension, and independence for individuals with 
autism, ultimately improving their quality of life. 
 
Facilitating effective decision-making in individuals with ASD during evacuations involves a multifaceted 
approach that includes adaption training, communication and environmental factors to their unique sensory 
and cognitive profiles, ensuring that they can navigate these challenges with confidence, although not 
overconfidence, so they may experience greater safety and efficacy. 
 
==PAGE:53==
 
 
52 
4.3.5 Adaptive Coping and Problem Solving in Evacuation Strategies for Individuals with ASD 
Coping through problem solving involves a structured approach to identifying, planning and implementing 
solutions during stressful situations like evacuations. Cognitive flexibility is often a challenge in ASD, which 
can make spontaneous problem-solving difficult. Therefore, it becomes important to focus on enhancing 
these skills. 
 
Individuals with ASD often struggle with the unpredictability of stressful situations making it difficult for them 
to adjust their behavior or strategies effectively. These individuals may require structured environments, 
clear communications, and the involvement of trained professionals to help them adapt to change. 
Structured environments that offer clear routines and expectations help reduce anxiety and support 
independence (Reynolds et al., 2011). Six core elements have been defined to have empirical support to be 
effective: Individualized support, systematic instruction, structured learning environment, specialized 
content, functional approach to problem behavior and family involvement (Iovannone et al., 2003). Tailoring 
interventions to the specific needs of the individual can be particularly beneficial in emergency situations, 
where a personalized approach may increase the likelihood of successful evacuation and adaption to the 
event. 
 
Teaching problem-solving strategies, where individuals are trained in how to break down a problem into 
manageable parts that are part of systematic instructions. They may be helped to think of possible solutions, 
and decide on the best action to take, which may help individuals with ASD to better cope with the 
unpredictability of evacuations (Kenworthy et al., 2008). Breaking down a problem could also be effectively 
used when doing scenario-based training. Here real-life scenarios or parts of them are re-enacted giving the 
individual the possibility to apply learned problem-solving skills in a controlled safe environment and as 
individualized support, which then can be transferred to real situations (Kandalaft et al., 2013). This could 
mimic some of the training individuals already receive at their schools. As one of the participants described 
a class he was taking, Social Studies, which are classes where they learn how to behave, how to talk to people, 
how to take care of personal hygiene and how to take care of personal thing in general. It could be relatively 
straight forward to include some parts of evacuation training and problem-solving strategies in Social Studies. 
Where are the safe areas, which part should be avoided and who can I approach for help, would be some of 
the examples.  
An interesting example of this can be seen with evacuation training with Japanese children emphasizing 
practical and inclusive strategies to enhance disaster preparedness and response. These sessions equip 
student with the knowledge of evacuation routes and necessary action during emergencies (Kitagawa, 2015). 
==PAGE:54==
 
 
53 
Additional, training programs in other disaster-prone countries, focused on child-centered choices during 
evacuations, aims to empower children by involving them in active decision-making and provides digital tools 
to reinforce lessons learned during training (TANAKA et al., 2022; Wierda, 2018) . 
 
Personalized support, tailored to meet the individual needs of a person, is another important part of adaptive 
coping and is often used at schools. This approach is especially beneficial for individuals with ASD, who may 
have unique sensory sensitivities, communication challenges, or difficulties with flexibilities and change. 
Personalized interventions can address these needs directly, improving outcomes in terms of both coping 
and psychological well-being. Programs such as Individualized Education Plans (IEPs) have been used in 
educational settings to provide tailored support for individuals with ASD, enhance learning outcomes and 
social integration (Blackwell & Rossetti, 2014).  In an evacuation context, personalized support could involve 
structured routines, visual aids, and specialized communication techniques to help individuals with ASD 
navigate the challenges of an emergency. 
 
With regards to social support from family or teachers, having trusted people available during an emergency 
can help individuals make clearer decisions and reduce feelings of isolation and fear (Ozbay et al., 2007). 
Research underscores that individuals with access to strong support networks often fare better in stressful 
circumstances, including evacuations, because of the emotional and practical resources they provide. Again, 
it was seen that the participants would often name a particular teacher, they would want to assist them if 
the needed to evacuate. Although this could be a double edge sword, if this teacher would not be available. 
Therefore back-ups should be considered. 
 
Effective communication strategies are essential for problem solving during evacuation, particular for autistic 
individuals who may experience challenges in understanding written or verbal instructions or even expressing 
their needs. Difficulties with social communication can lead to confusion, with regards to adaptive coping, 
during evacuations. Verbal instructions may be misinterpreted, causing delays in response (C. Mazefsky et 
al., 2018). Non-verbal aids like pictograms or visual evacuation maps can help in the understanding of what 
to do without relying on verbal cues.  
 
Similarly implementing social stories or role-playing exercises may also prepare them for emergency 
scenarios, reducing uncertainty (Velasco et al., 2012). It has also been reported that social stories of 
situations, events or activity, which include specific information about what to expect and why, may assist 
both children and adults in acquiring new skills (Karkhaneh et al., 2010; Samuels & Stansfield, 2012). Although 
==PAGE:55==
 
 
54 
there seems to be an agreement, that the social stories need to be relevant for the individual and target the 
specific deficits in the individual, as previously stated. Social stories were quite common among the students, 
but some of them were not related to real life, but often related to computer games they were playing. Some 
of their perceived skills were most likely from scenes replayed from videogames, such as seeing evacuation 
as a possibility of jumping through windows or out from windows at high altitude. Others were of more 
realistic nature where one participant told the interviewer that he had been told it was better to be in a place 
where there were less things that could burn or catch fire. Another argued that a science room would also 
be a good place to be, as they had equipment to put fires out, which they had been told stories about. 
 
When communicating either verbal, non-verbal, personal or on a group level it is important that it is done in 
a  clear and consistent language, with an emphasis on direct sentences and avoiding idiomatic expressions 
(such as `clear out` or `head for the hills`, should be exchanged for: we need to get out of the house now) or 
proverbs (better to lose everything and save a life, than to risk it all to save things), can help the individual 
better comprehend instructions (Morsanyi & Stamenković, 2021). This also became evident in the interviews 
where the interviewer made this mistake a couple of times, which lead to misunderstandings. When the 
mistake is noticed it is important to ask what the person understands and rectify the mistakes made in 
communication, so the person understands the specific meaning and the person communicating to the 
individual with ASD learns from the mistakes. 
 
The person-environment fit model (PE Model) who was developed by Lawton and Nahemow (Lawton & 
Nahemow, 1973),  emphasizes the interaction between individual characteristics (person) and the evacuation 
environment. A good fit between these elements fosters adaptation. In this context it is not only the 
individual, but also caregivers and peers (Howlin et al., 1999). Training sessions for caregivers and first 
responders on how to effectively support individuals with ASD during evacuations, may increase the chance 
of successful interaction in stressful situations. This might be done as role playing exercises. In Denmark all 
first responders do weekly training sessions for special emergencies, such as water rescue, rescues in high 
altitude, ladder evacuations etc. . It does not seem as if there is an emphasis on exercises with humans as 
even their own body (Beredskabsstyrelsen) have called for more training and testing of emergencies.   
Roleplaying emergencies, with all individuals, not only individuals with ASD, as part of a scheduled training, 
should be seen as important as other training sessions and could also give the first responders a good chance 
to learn more about how individuals with ASD, as well as neurotypical persons, might behave during 
emergencies. The PE model suggest that individuals with emergency preparedness will adapt better to 
challenging environments. Additionally, creating evacuation plans that consider sensory sensitivities and 
==PAGE:56==
 
 
55 
communication needs can improve adaptation for individuals with ASD with the interaction of first 
responders.  
 
Research have also shown that an induvial with ASD often demonstrate enhanced visual processing 
capabilities (Jolliffe & Baron-Cohen, 1997). Autist also generally have a high degree of pattern recognition. 
Pictograms capitalize on these strengths by presenting information in a visual form that is easier to 
comprehend and remember. This makes pictograms an effective way of communication with autist, as they 
are visual representations of concepts, objects, or actions, making them easier to understand for individuals 
who may have difficulties with verbal communication or processing language. By using pictograms, educators 
or therapists can make information more accessible and meaningful. This would be of aid, as visual support 
could play an important role in facilitating effective decision making for autistic individuals during evacuation. 
Alternative communication methods such as visual aids, which could be maps, pictograms, diagrams, and 
written instructions translated to speech, to convey important information about evacuation routes, 
assembly points, and safety procedures. These visual supports provide concrete and recognizable references 
that can help autistic individuals navigate evacuation procedures more confidently and independently. 
 
As this section concludes the discussion on the facilitators and strategies enhancing psychological adaptation 
and evacuation outcomes for individuals with ASD, it is important to recognizing the variability in autistic 
strengths, challenges and preferences and that more focus on individualized planning and preparation 
strategies tailored to each person’s unique needs would be beneficial.  
 
The approaches ranging from medical and social models of disability to cognitive an emotional adaptation 
underscores the necessity for training, personalized and sensitivity planning in emergency responses. 
 
This could also involve personalized assessment to identify specific sensory sensitivities, communication 
styles, and coping strategies, and develop customized evacuation plans that address these factors. By 
empowering individuals with personalized tools and supports, it may be possible to enhance their confidence 
and autonomy not only during evacuations, but maybe more generally. 
 
To ensure effective evacuations scenarios a collaborate partnership among relevant stakeholders, such as 
emergency responders, caregivers, and lawmakers is required. First, sharing knowledge about autism, 
exchange best practice, and develop evidence-based interventions that promote safety and well-being of 
autistic individuals during emergencies. Fostering collaboration among stakeholders can contribute to the 
==PAGE:57==
 
 
56 
creation of more supportive and resilient evacuation systems that to a higher degree meets the diverse needs 
of all community members. 
 
4.4 Implications of Findings for Theory and Practice  
The research findings have several implications for both theoretical models and practical applications in the 
field of psychology and emergency management, particularly concerning individuals with ASD during 
evacuations.  
4.4.1 Theoretical Contributions 
The findings reinforce Lazarus and Folkman’s theory on cognitive appraisal and stress, highlighting that 
individuals with ASD might appraise stressful situations, like evacuations, differently due to sensory 
sensitivities and social communication challenges (Lazarus, 1974). This may lead to an overestimation of 
threats or misinterpretations of social cues, which complicates their responses to emergencies. These results 
suggest a need for models of cognitive appraisal to consider sensory and cognitive processing differences 
inherent in ASD.  
 
The research underscores the intricate interplay between cognitive biases and emergency response behavior 
in individuals with ASD. It illuminates how the Donning-Kruger effect and anchoring bias can critically impair 
judgement before or during evacuations, leading to overestimations of one’s capabilities (Kahneman & 
Tversky, 2013; Kruger et al., 1999), a discovery that does not seem to have been reported before. This 
research also explores the impact of social influence on risk perception, suggesting that overprotective 
behavior with regards to individuals with ASD, by caregivers could reinforce these cognitive biases, 
potentially exacerbating the challenges faced during emergencies (Arslan et al., 2023; Mileti & Sorensen, 
1990). Addressing these biases  through improved training and awareness could enhance the safety and 
efficacy of evacuation procedures, contributing to better emergency preparedness and response strategies. 
 
Although the study also enhances the understanding of adaptive coping within ASD, illustrating that routine 
and predictability, significantly aid in stress management for this group. This finding can influence theoretical 
framework around coping strategies, suggesting they be tailored to incorporate the cognitive and sensory 
profiles of individuals with ASD, providing a more nuanced understanding resilience in diverse populations. 
 
4.4.2 Practical Implications 
The insight into the social model, sensory sensitivities and cognitive processing in ASD should give way for 
the development of evacuation protocols that are more inclusive (Barnes, 2019). This could involve the 
==PAGE:58==
 
 
57 
integrations of sensory friendly alerts and clear visual communication strategies that accommodate the 
unique needs of individuals with ASD, but maybe even more important it should consider evacuation 
exercises for all. Regular evacuation exercises may help mitigate the stress and confusion often experienced 
during evacuations, thereby enhancing safety and effectiveness. 
 
There is also a clear indication for more information and understanding of ASD in evacuation, as well as the 
need of targeted training programs for emergency responders and educators that focus on the specific 
challenges and possibilities of individuals with ASD in high-stress situations. These programs should 
emphasize sensory triggers and use specific communication tactics that re likely to be more effective during 
interventions with this population. 
 
There is also a need for policy maker to get involved, as this population is expanding. They should use these 
findings to advocate for regulations that ensure emergency preparedness plans are inclusive of neurodiverse 
individuals. Policies should mandate that public spaces and institutions like schools have evacuations plans 
that specifically address the needs of individuals with ASD, including training for staff to assist effectively in 
emergencies. 
 
The above implications are just a few of the implications that is necessary and highlights the necessity for a 
multidisciplinary approach that bridges theory with practical applications, ensuring that individuals with ASD 
receive the support they need before and during evacuations and other emergency scenarios. By integrating 
these insights into practice, it may be more possible to safeguard and support one of the more vulnerable 
populations in our communities during crisis. 
 
4.5 Limitations 
The limitation section acknowledges the constraints that might affect the interpretation and generalizability 
of the research findings. It is essential to consider these limitations to understand the extent to which the 
results can be applied beyond the study’s specific context. This includes recognizing potential biases, sample 
related constraints, and other factors that may influence the outcomes. Together, the scope and limitations 
provide a comprehensive framework for understanding the study’s contribution and the context in which the 
findings should be interpreted.  
 
In considering the limitations of this study, it is important to address the potential challenges to the 
generalizability of the findings. This work explores the complex area of psychological adaptation in individuals 
==PAGE:59==
 
 
58 
with ASD during evacuations, particularly focusing on enhancing adaptive strategies and addressing 
perceptual barriers. While the study contributes valuable insight into this unexplored area, several areas 
must be acknowledged to contextualize the findings. 
1. Available time: 
Time was a specific limitation to this study, as it is with most- Although the foundation for this study 
would be excellent for a more in-depth study, as the information is missing in research. 
2. Sample size and diversity: 
There was only a limited number of participants available for this study. Most schools are very wary 
to include individuals with ASD in a study as they perceive it might affect them negatively. This will 
restrict the generalizability of the findings. The demographic characteristics of the sample, such as 
gender, age range, cognitive ability, geographical location, and level of diagnosis among participants, 
is not representative of a broader population of individuals with ASD. A larger more diverse sample, 
where women were also included, would allow for a more robust conclusions and the ability to 
generalize findings to a wider community. 
Moreover, the reliance on self-reported experiences introduces additional limitations. Participants’ 
recollection of past events may be affected by recall bias, where accuracy of their memories is 
compromised, especially when recalling specific details (Coughlin, 1990). This issue is compounded 
by potential limitation in emotional self-awareness (Huggins et al., 2021), as participants might not 
be fully aware of all the factors that influence their experiences. These factors could lead to an 
incomplete or skewed representation of the true nature of the experiences being studied. 
3. Methodological constraints: 
The reliance on qualitative methods, while providing depth and nuanced understanding. Limits the 
ability to quantify the impact of identified factors on psychological adaptation during evacuations. 
The subjective nature of thematic analysis and the potential for researcher bias during data 
interpretation also pose challenges to the objectivity of the findings. The quantitative findings from 
the questionaries, had to be excluded as it would be too easy to identify the students if they were 
published. They were used, but not visually displayed. 
It was not possible to conduct evacuation drills, but this would have been preferable. 
4. Scope of emergency situation: 
The scope of the study was restricted to a single type of emergency – fire emergencies. By excluding 
other types of emergencies, the study’s findings may not be applicable to situations involving 
different emergency contexts, which might elicit different responses or challenges for individuals on 
the autism spectrum. This focus on fire emergencies alone, means that the study does not provide 
==PAGE:60==
 
 
59 
insights into how individuals might react or cope with other emergency scenarios, thereby limiting 
the application of findings to other emergency situations, which may have unique sensory and 
cognitive demands. 
5. Measurement of psychological adaptation: 
The study’s conceptualization and measurement of psychological adaptation rely heavily on self-
reported data, which can be influenced by participants current mood, memory biases, and personal 
insights. Objective measures of stress, coping, and adaptation were not investigated or utilize. If so 
they might have provided a more comprehensive understanding of the physiological and 
psychological impact of imagined evacuations. 
6. Temporal aspects: 
The design of the study does not allow for the examination of changes over time in psychological 
adaptation. Longitudinal studies of evacuation drills would be needed to understand how adaptive 
strategies evolve and how interventions might impact long-term outcomes in evacuation scenarios. 
7. Intervention efficacy: 
While the study proposes various adaptive strategies and interventions, these recommendations 
have not been empirically tested within the research. The effectiveness of these interventions 
remains theoretical without practical validation through experimental or applied research. 
8. Social and environmental factors: 
The study does not explore the influence of social, cultural, and environmental factors that could 
affect the evacuation experience. These factors may include community awareness, societal 
attitudes towards perceived disability, and physical accessibility of evacuation environments. 
 
Closing the limitation section, it is important to recognize the constraints that may influence the robustness 
of this study’s conclusions. While the insights gathered offer valuable information and directions for future 
research and immediate application, the limitations arising from the sample size, methodological choices, 
and scope of evacuation situations addressed here necessitates a cautious approach to generalizing these 
findings. Future studies could enhance understanding by expanding the types of emergencies analyzed, 
employ a mixed methods approach to incorporate both qualitative depth and qualitative breadth, and 
broadening the participant pool to capture a more diverse spectrum of experience within the autism 
community. This consideration of limitations not only clarifies the current study’s boundaries but also maps 
out essential directions for subsequent research efforts. 
==PAGE:61==
 
 
60 
5.0 Conclusion 
The purpose of this thesis was to explore the complex dynamics of psychological adaptation for individuals 
with ASD in evacuation situations. Through a mixed-methods analysis, significant barriers that hinder 
successful evacuation for individuals with ASD, such as evacuation preparedness, sensory sensitivities, 
communication difficulties, and challenges in understanding social interaction and communication, was 
investigated. Similarly, facilitators that will support evacuation and adaptation, such as evacuation drills, 
personalized support, sensory friendly environments, and structured communication strategies have been 
outlined. 
 
The thesis recognizes that the neurological differences in ASD are the results of normal variations in the 
human genome and views the differences not as deficits, but as natural variations that should be respected 
and accepted as any other human variation, just as the Social Model of Disability suggests. This means that 
when neurotypical individuals are trained in evacuation drills, so should neurodivergent. There might be 
many ways of practicing, but practice in general should be preferred. So, this is where it should all begin, 
everyone, no matter if they are neurotypical or neurodiverse, should have the opportunity to practice 
evacuation. That being said:  
 
The findings underline the importance of integrating neurodiversity into emergency planning and 
management. Traditional evacuation protocols often overlook the unique needs of neurodivergent 
individuals, such as it was done with physical disabilities for centuries, leading to strategies that are not only 
ineffective but also potential harmful in creating unnecessary difficulties during evacuation. The research 
highlights that with the right supports or even just practice, in place, individuals with ASD may significantly 
improve their ability to cope with demands of an evacuations, suggesting a shift towards more inclusive 
evacuation practices. 
 
The findings also demonstrate the critical role of engagement with ASD communities in developing these 
strategies. Involvement of individuals with ASD in the planning and testing of evacuation procedures ensure 
that the measures are not only theoretically sound but also practically applicable. An approach like that 
would enhance the relevance and effectiveness of evacuation protocols but also empower individuals with 
ASD, acknowledging their agency and competence.  
 
In conclusion, the study advocates for a paradigm shift in the way evacuation is approached for individuals 
with ASD. It calls for a move away from the overprotective strategy of not including individuals with ASD in 
==PAGE:62==
 
 
61 
evacuation exercises. Including both neurotypical and neurodivergent individuals, but also a call to move 
away from the one-size-fits-all strategy to a more nuanced and personalized approach that considers the 
broad spectrum of needs in the ASD community. By implementing some of the findings of this research, 
policymakers, emergency planners fire and rescue may ensure that evacuation procedures are less anxiety 
inducing, more accessible, reducing potential harm and promoting the safety and well-being of individuals 
with ASD. This approach not only benefits individuals with ASD but may also enhance overall resilience and 
prepare societies to manage evacuation more effectively and compassionately. 
 
As continuing advances are made, it is important that ongoing research continues to refine these strategies, 
supported also by advances in technology and maybe even a deeper understanding of ASD in emergency 
situations. Continuous collaboration with the ASD community and researchers will be essential in helping 
implementing evacuation exercises and crafting emergency protocols that are both effective and respectful 
of the needs of all individuals. 
 
2024 
Heidi Kjøgx

Spørgeskema omkring brandsikkerhed og evakuering 
 
Vi er i gang med en undersøgelse som skal udforske mulighederne for at øge brandsikkerhed og 
evakueringsmuligheder for neurodivergente på autismespektret. 
 
Vi vil derfor gerne starte med at sige tak for at du deltager i dette spørgeskema omkring brandsikkerhed og 
evakuering. Dit svar vil være med til at skabe en bedre forståelse for de behov der er og være med til at 
udvikle strategier for at sikre en større sikkerhed i forbindelse med evakuering.

Appendix 2: Participant Information and Consent 
 
 
==PAGE:83==
 
 
82 
Deltager information og samtykke 
Undersøgelse af psykologisk Blpasning i forbindelse med evakuering. 
Vi undersøger hvordan mennesker håndterer stress og usikkerhed under evakueringer, specielt personer på  
autisme spektret. Din deltagelse kan hjælpe os med at forstå, hvad der hjælper folk med at klare sig godt 
under evakueringer. Denne viden kan bruges til at udvikle bedre planer og støtte til alle i forbindelse med 
evakueringer og andre nødsituationer. 
Formål og nyIe af undersøgelsen 
Dette studie undersøger, hvilken viden mennesker på autisme spektret har omkring evakuering og hvordan 
de oplever evakueringer, samt hvad der kan hjælpe dem med at tilpasse sig. Vi håber på at kunne identificere 
både udfordringer og styrker, der påvirker den psykiske tilstand i forbindelse med evakuering. 
Din deltagelse kan bidrage med at forbedre evakuerings procedurer for alle mennesker, herunder dem på 
autisme spektret. Forskningen kan før til bedre støtte og information under evakuering, hvilket kan reducere 
stress og angst og eventuelle følgevirkninger af disse tilstande efterfølgende. 
Studiet laves i samarbejde med DTU-Danmarks Tekniske Universitet. 
Bivirkninger og risici 
Deltagelse kræver ca. 1-2 timer til et interview. Du kan muligvis opleve følelser som ubehag hvis du tidligere 
har haft dårlige oplevelser med evakuering, når du taler om disse oplevelser. Vi vil sørge for en støttende 
atmosfære og pauser under interviewet, hvis det er nødvendigt. Du kan altid vælge at trække dig fra 
interviewet på ethvert tidspunkt. 
Udelukkelse fra og aKrydelse af forsøg 
Du kan ikke deltage i studiet, hvis du ikke har en autisme diagnose. Du kan frit trække dig fra studiet på 
ethvert tidspunkt uden konsekvenser. Forskeren kan vælge at afbryde din deltagelse, hvis de mener, det ikke 
er i din bedste interesse. 
Plan for forsøget 
Du vil deltage i et enkelt interview, der stilles af en erfaren psykolog. Interviewet vil handle om dine 
eventuelle erfaringer med evakuering og din generelle følelse af tryghed i nødsituationer. Interviewet forgår 
på et tidspunkt og sted, der passer dig bedst. Du er velkommen til at tage en kendt person med, hvis du har 
behov for det. 
Kontaktperson 
For spørgsmål om studiet kan du kontakte hovedforskeren Psykolog Heidi Kjøgx på 51680411 eller 
heidi.kjogx@gmail.com.  
Resultater 
Studiet er ikke tænkt til umiddelbar publicering, men kan blive delt med relevante organisationer der 
arbejder med evakuering og autisme. Alle oplysninger vil blive anonymiseret i rapporter og publikationer. 
Vi håber du vil overveje at deltage i studiet. Læs venligst deltagerinformationen grundigt igen, hvis du har 
spørgsmål, er du velkommen til at kontakte os. 
(S1)

Det videnskabs etiske komitésystem 2024

Erklæring fra forsøgspersonen: 
Jeg har fået skriftlig og mundtlig information og jeg ved nok om formål, metode, fordele og  
ulemper til at sige ja til at deltage.  
Jeg ved, at det er frivilligt at deltage, og at jeg altid kan trække mit samtykke tilbage uden at  
miste mine nuværende eller fremtidige rettigheder til behandling.   
Jeg giver samtykke til, at deltage i forskningsprojektet, og har fået en kopi af dette samtykkeark samt en 
kopi af den skriftlige information om projektet til eget brug.
